                     Single tranche

                     The proposed Sustainable Development DPLProgram(comprising
                     of three operations, SusDevDPL I,    SusDevDPL 11and SusDevDPL
                     111)will support the government of Colombia's efforts to move
                     towards achievement of the MillenniumDevelopment Goals
                     (MDGs), particularly Goal 7 which seeks to ensureenvironmental
                     sustainability. Specifically, the programto be supported has the
                     followingobjectives: (i)   improving the effectiveness and efficiency
                     of the National Environmental System (SINA); and (ii)     integrating
                     principles of sustainable development into key sectors, with a
                     particular emphasis on protectingthe most vulnerable groups.
                     The SusDevDPL Programwould improve environmental
                     management, particularly inurban areas. The programsupports
                     developing a results-based framework for planning and monitoring
                     progress of the SINA towards goals directly linked with sustainable
                     development and achievement of the MDGs; improvements in inter-
                     institutionalcoordination and increased public participationin
                     environmental decision-making; and preparationof critical policies
                     and laws related to air quality, water quality, solid waste
                     management, and environmental licensing.

                     The SusDevDPL Programhas clear poverty implications, given that
                     environmental degradation disproportionately affects the poor and
                     most vulnerable populations, and thus increases inequality.

Improvements in air and water quality in urban centers will lead to
decreasedmorbidity and mortality from diarrheal diseases, acute
respiratory illnesses and other health effects, and allow for
reductions in avertive expenditures to address healthrisks.
Increased accountability, transparency and improved public
participation will provide the most vulnerable groups a voice in
environmental decision-making.
Several risks have been identified: (i)the overall political context,
namely the internalconflict and violence associatedwith illegal
drugactivity, which could negatively affect the fiscal and
macroeconomic environment while aggravating the social situation,
and the current debate about a secondPresidential term, which may
affect the pace of the implementationof difficult reforms; (ii)  fiscal
pressures, which have lead to cuts in spending related to
environmental programs; (iii)  the external economic and political
shocks due to the region's susceptibility to regional political or
economic instability or further deterioration of the terms of trade;
(iv) coordination risks, given that significant multi-sectoral
collaboration i s neededto mainstreamimprovedenvironmental
managementinto key sectors; and (v) institutional risks, given that
there may be vested interests (e.g., pollution-intensive industries,
Regional Autonomous Corporations) inderailing the reform
process.

Throughout the reform program, several actions are being
undertaken to mitigate the above-mentioned risks. These include:
(i)complementary actions in other sectors to support the
development of a stronger foundation for peace and reforms to
liberateresources to be used for the transition to a post-conflict
Colombia; (ii)  focused efforts to improve the effectiveness and
efficiency of investments inthe SINA; (iii)   complementary actions
supported by IMF,Bank, and IDBprograms to mitigate the
contagion effects from regional crises; (iv) institutional
strengthening of SINA institutions, includingthe NationalPlanning
Department, to support consensus-building inenvironmental
decision-making within priority sectors; and (v) Bank support via
the complementary Sustainable Developmentinvestment project
will support consultation, monitoringand evaluation of SINA
efforts, and targeted investments aimed at strengthening
environmentalmanagement inpriority sectors, and a communicatior
program to publicly disseminate environmental information.

                         IBRDPROGRAMDOCUMENTFORA
       PROPOSEDPROGRAMMATICDEVELOPMENTPOLICYLOANFOR
                            SUSTAINABLEDEVELOPMENT
                          TO THE REPUBLICOF COLOMBIA

1.      Environment and natural resources are integral to sustainable growth and poverty
reduction inColombia. Over the past two decades, as will be discussed below, Colombia has
taken numerous policy initiatives and built up its institutional capacity to protect and manage
its natural resources. However, it continues to face serious challenges in terms of the
degradation of its land, air, and water resources, compromising its potential for sustainable
economic growth. The overarching objective of the proposed SustainableDevelopment DPL
Program (comprising of three operations, SusDevDPL I,       SusDevDPL IIand SusDevDPL m)
i s to support the government of Colombia's efforts to move towards achievement of the
Millennium Development Goals (MDGs), particularly Goal 7 which seeks to ensure
environmental sustainability, Target 9 which aims to integrate the principles of sustainable
development into country policies and programs, Target 10 which aims to reduce the
proportion of people without sustainable access to safe drinking water and basic sanitation,
and Target 11which aims to achieve a significant improvement inthe lives of the urban poor,
Furthermore, by addressing urban environmental issues affecting the most vulnerable
populations (e.g., air pollution, and inadequate water supply, sanitation and hygiene), the
program supports achievement of Goal 4 which seeks to reduce child mortality through
addressing principal threats to children under five (e.g., morbidity and mortality related to
respiratory illnesses and diarrheal diseases). Specifically, the program to be supportedhas the
following objectives: (a) improving the effectiveness and efficiency of the National
Environmental System (Sistema Nacional Ambiental, SINA) in Colombia; and (b) integrating
principles of sustainable development into key sectors, with a particular emphasis on
protecting the most vulnerable groups. (See Box 1.) This Program Document proposes a
single-tranche Programmatic Development Policy Loan for Sustainable Development of
US$150million to the Republic of Colombia, to be implemented by the Ministry of Finance
andPublic Credit (Ministerio de Hacienda y Crkdito Publico, MHCP) with support from the
Ministry of Environment, Housing and Regional Development (Ministerio de Ambiente,
Vivienda y Desarrollo Territorial, MAVDT) and the National Planning Department
(DepartamentoNacional de Planeacidn, DNP).

2.      This report i s organized in the following way: Chapter Idescribes the challenges
facing Colombia, a lower middle-income nation which suffers significant environmental
costs related in large part to inadequate water supply, sanitation and hygiene; natural
disasters; urban air pollution; and agricultural land degradation. Chapter IIpresents the
macroeconomicenvironment, while Chapter IUpresentsthe government's reform agenda and
discusses the measures it has taken to meet the challenges it faces. Chapter I V details the
rational for the Bank's involvement in the reform efforts in Colombia and the relation with
the 2003-2007 Country Assistance Strategy, the Country Environmental Analysis (CEA)
which was carried out inparallel with preparation of the SusDevDPL, and the lessons learned
from similar operations in Mexico and Brazil. Chapter V reviews the specific objectives of
the SusDevDPL program, prior actions and triggers for the second operation. Finally,
Chapter VI addresses operational issuesrelated to the SusDevDPL program.


                                                3

                          Box 1. Elementsof Colombia's NationalEnvironmentalSystem

    Law 99 of 1993 created Colombia's Ministryof Environment (now MAVDT) to consolidate many of the principal
environmental management functions dispersedthroughout various branchesof national government, and to provide a
means of coordinating environmental managementinboth the public and private sectors; similarly, the law created the
NationalEnvironmental System (SINA). The law's drafters intended SINA to provide stakeholders inthe environmental
sector with a coordinated mechanism for protectingthe environment. Consistent with the Constitution of 1991,this
managementsystem was to be decentralized, democratic and participatory. Law 99 defines SINA as a "set of
orientations, norms, activities, resources, programs and institutionsthat allow the implementationof general
environmental principles" oriented around a model of sustainable development. SINA may be thought of as a
managementsystem made up of actors, coordinationand planning mechanisms, mechanisms for public participation, legal
norms, mechanisms for implementing and enforcing policy, and financial resources. These SINA actors include:

                         Key government environmental      Other govt. institutionswith environmental
                         authorities                       responsibilities
                              MAVDT                             Departmentof NationalPlanning(DNP)
                              CARsandAAUs                       Govemmentoversightinstitutions
                              NationalPark System               Ministriesother than MAVTD
                                                                ResearchInstitutes
                         Territorial authorities                NationalEnvironmentalCouncil
                              Departments                       TechnicalAdvisory Council
                              Municipalities
                           4  Territoriesof Indigenous     Privateresearch institutions
                              Peop1es
                                                           Civil society
                                                                Environmentalnon-governmental
                                                                organizations
                                                                Privatefirms andfarms

    Law 99 organized SINA around a number of guiding principles, including: (1) Economic and social development in
Colombia will be guided by the goal of sustainabledevelopment laid out inthe UnitedNations Conference on
Environment and Development (UNCED), held in Rio de Janeiro in 1992;(2)Biodiversity must be protected and should
only be exploited in a sustainable manner; (3) Human consumption has priority among water uses; (4) Environmental
policy will be basedon the scientific evidence. However, the lack of scientific evidence cannot be used as a rationalefor
not acting to prevent serious irreversibleharm; (5) Environmental policy will rely on the use of economic instruments to
incorporate environmental costs as a means of preserving the environment and conserving renewable natural resources;
(6)Environmental protectionis a coordinated task between the state, community, NGOs, and the private sector. The state
will support the development of environmental NGOs and may delegate some governmental functions to them; and (7)
Environmental impact studies will be the basic instrument for deciding whether to engage in activities that may
significantly affect the environment.

    Withinthe SINA, while MAVDThas responsibility for planning national environmentalpolicy, regional
environmental authorities, including Regional Autonomous Corporations (CARs) and Urban Environmental Authorities
(AAUs) have responsibilityfor implementing it. That said, CARSand AAUs are largely autonomous entities and
determine their own regional priorities and investment programs. Rulemaking adheres to the subsidiary principle-that    is,
requirements adopted by lower levels of government cannot be weaker than nor weaken those of higher levels of
government. However, the capacity of CARSto enforce existing legislation.and decrees is relatively poor and
inconsistent across CARs.

    SINA instruments of governance and management include legal norms such as environmental laws, presidential
decrees and enforcement actions, together with other governance instruments such as environmental licensing, command
and control regulations, voluntary agreements, and economic instruments such as effluent fees.




                                                                 4

                                  Box 2. Colombia's Country EnvironmentalAnalysis

    Country Environmental Analysis (CEA) makes it possible to evaluate systematically the environmental priorities of
development in client countries, the environmental implications of key policies, and countries' capacity to address their
priorities. A CEA was conducted in Colombia in parallel with preparation of the SusDevDPL, to provide the govemment
with an analytical basis for decision-making on how best to improve institutional structures for environmental
management as it moves forward with reforms to the SINA. By achieving this objective, the CEA provided a basis for the
structure of the SusDevDPL and Sustainable Development Investment project. Comerstones of the CEA include: (i)               an
assessment of the cost o f environmental degradation in Colombia; (ii)     a broad assessment of institutional capacity for
environmental management in Colombia; (iii)an assessment of the effectiveness and efficiency of the existing policy,
legislative and regulatory framework to address environmental concems; and (iv) a public environmental expenditure
review. Furthermore, a broad survey of societal perceptions was carried out to assist the government in identifying the
country's priority environmentalconcems, as well as the sectors from which they stem.
     As described in detail in paragraphs 87-88 below, the findings of the CEA servedto identify the priority areas which
would be targeted in the SusDevDPL.            A study on the "Cost of Environmental Damage: A Socio-Economic and
Environmental Health Risk Assessment" provides estimates of social and economic costs of environmental damage in
Colombia. Costs are presented for a number of environmental categories: (i) sanitation and hygiene; (ii)
                                                                                   water,                               natural
disasters; (iii)urban air pollution; (iv) soil erosion and salinization; and (v) indoor air pollution. Data limitations
prevented estimation of degradation costs at the national level for coastal zones (fisheries and tourism losses), wetlands,
noise pollution, biodiversity, protected areas and inappropriate solid waste disposal and inadequate industrial and hospital
waste management. It is however doubtful that aggregate costs of degradation and health risks arising from these issues
are anywhere close to the costs associatedwith water, sanitation, and hygiene, land degradation, and indoor and urban air
pollution. The mean estimated annual cost of environmental damage totals approximately seven trillion Colombian Pesos
per year, totaling 3.7 percent of GDP. The highest cost category is inadequate water supply, sanitation and hygiene;
followed by natural disasters; urban air pollution; agricultural land degradation; and indoor air pollution somewhat lower.
Principal costs in urban areas relate to outdoor air pollution together with water supply & sanitation; in rural areas, the
principal costs relate to agricultural land degradation, and to a much lower extent, water supply and sanitation, and indoor
air pollution.
     The "Assessment of Colombia's National Environmental System (SINA)" study reviewed the effectiveness of SINA
institutions, namely MAVDT, the CARs, and municipal agencies as well as their institutional capacity to implement and
enforce policies and regulations in priority areas. Principal problems identified in the study include inter alia: (i)
Inadequate regulations. For example, command-and-control emissions standards have sometimes been adopted from
more developed countries with little or no modification; (ii)  Limited technical capacity in MAVDT and some CARs; (iii)
Conditions which are conducive to regulatory capture. Evidence suggests that interest groups exert undue influence on
the activities of environmental authorities at both the national and regional levels, so that instead of acting to further social
welfare, they act to further the interests of select groups; (iv) Inadequate enforcement. For example, of the effluent fees
that CARs charge to polluters, only one-third were actually collected over a five-year period (1997-2002); (v) Inadequate
data on environmental quality and institutional performance; (vi) Lack of priority setting across environmental sub-sectors
and programs; and (vii) Inadequate mechanisms for public participation.
     The "Colombian Environmental Priorities" study included a survey of more than 2,600 Colombians throughout the
country, including numerous sectors, governmental authorities, and civil society representatives.             Air pollution is
identified as a very serious problem by 79 percent of persons surveyed, followed by global environmental problems
(73%), deforestation (65%), noise pollution (65%) and vulnerability to natural disasters (65%). However, the study
illustrates significant differences in priorities between groups surveyed; for instance, whereas low income groups identify
air pollution, noise pollution and vulnerability to natural disasters as major problems, upper income groups perceive the
loss of global environmental resources (e.g., biodiversity, global warming) and inappropriate land use in urban areas as
principal problems.
     These studies and others carried out as part of the CEA reflect a potential bias in environmental expenditures in
Colombia; whereas over 70 percent of Colombia's population lives in urban areas, environmental expenditures focus
disproportionately on natural resource issues, forestry and biodiversity. In recognition of this potential "green bias" and
the lack of a cohesive program to focus upon the needs of Colombia's most vulnerable groups, the government requested
that the SusDevDPL and complementary investment project focus on issues related to the quality of life of the urban poor
and the broader issues of social, economic, and environmentally sustainable development as they affect the growing
number of poor people living inand around towns and cities.
                                                                  5

                                  I. INTRODUCTION

BACKGROUNDAND RATIONALE
3.      Environmental degradation disproportionately affects poor people's health and
productivity and thus increases inequality within society. Urban environmental problems
are most frequently threats to present or future human well-being, resultingfrom human-
induced damage to the physical environment, originating inurban areas. This includes inter
alia: (i) localized environmental health problems such as inadequate household water,
sanitation and hygiene and indoor air pollution; (ii)   city-regional environmental problems
such as ambient air pollution, inadequate waste management and pollution of rivers, lakes
and coastal areas; (iii)  extra-urban impacts of urban activities such as natural resource
degradation / depletion in surrounding areas together with global environmental problems
(e.g., global climate change).

4.       On a global scale, lack of access to clean water and sanitation, inadequate hygiene,
and indoor air pollution are among the principal causes of illness anddeath, predominantly
affecting children and women inpoor families. Modern hazards such as urban air pollution
and exposure to hazardous wastes add to these traditional risks.            The five highest
environmental problems in terms of the cost of environmental degradation for Colombia
amount to 3.7 percent of GDP. These costs are mainly linked with increasedmortality and
morbidity and decreased productivity associated with the following environmental issues,
namely (ii)  inadequate water supply, sanitation and hygiene; (ii) disasters; (iii)
                                                                    natural               poor
urban air quality, (iv) soil erosion and salinization; and (v) the use of fuel wood in rural
households which results inpoor indoor air quality.

5.       The proposed loan i s the first in an expected series of quick-disbursing loans aimed
at supporting the government of Colombia's interdisciplinary sustainable development
program. This first loan supports initial actions that are vital steps in the government's
program. These actions include: developing a results-based framework for planning and
monitoring progress of the SINA towards goals directly linked with sustainable
development and achievement of the MDGs; improvements in inter-institutional
coordination and increased public participation in environmental decision-making through
changes in the existing Technical Advisory Council; preparation of critical policies that
address urban environmental issues; and preparation of a water resource management law,
an action program for establishing a national policy relating to air pollution control, and
decrees related to environmental licensing and solid waste management.

THE ENVIRONMENTAL CHALLENGESINCOLOMBIA
6.       Over the past three decades, Colombia has taken numerous policy initiatives and
built up its institutional capacity to protect and manage its natural resources. Colombia's
first CAR, the Regional Autonomous Corporation of the Valle del Cauca, was created in
 1954 to promote integrated economic development in that region.                    Significant
environmental regulations approved in the 1960s and 1970s included the 1969 Forestry
Law, a 1977 statute creating the National Park System, and a 1973 statute covering flora



                                                  6

and fauna. B y far the most important new legislation, however, was the 1974 National
Natural Renewable Resources and Environmental Protection Code, a comprehensive
statute, which remains one of the principal pillars of Colombian natural resource and
environmental law. The Code's 340 articles cover water, air, solid and hazardous waste,
soil, flora, and fauna. The Code was one of the first environmental protection laws in the
world to incorporate pollution fees and environmental impact assessments. During the
1980s, Colombia designed and implemented air and water pollution control regulations.
The 1991 Constitution and Law 99 of 1993 markedthe formation of both the SINA, which
defined the overall framework for environmental management in the country, and the
Ministry of Environment.

7.       More recently, the government has attained significant environmental goals such as
establishing national natural parks (8.5% of the national territory) and forestry reserves
(more than 15% of the national territory), and phasing out leaded gasoline. At the local
level, achievements have likewise been impressive. For example, in the late 1990s the
municipal government in Bogota implemented a comprehensive program to improve the
quality of life in the city that included (i)          shifting from a program of building roads for
automobiles only to a model which includes an efficient public transport system, pedestrian
walkways and bicycle paths; (ii)           increasing the enrollment of students in schools by more
than 30 percent; and (iii)      buildingor rebuilding 1,243 parks which are visitedby more than
1.5 million people annually.

8.       However, despite these achievements, Colombia continues to face challenges in
terms of degradation of its land, air, and water resources, compromising its potential for
sustainable economic growth. A recent analysis of the costs of environmental degradation
indicates that the most significant costs facing Colombia are associated with urban air
pollution, as well as inadequate water, sanitation and hygiene, where costs are estimated at
2,730 and 2,140 billion pesos per year, respectively. In addition, natural disasters and
indoor air pollution pose considerable costs, estimated respectively per year at 2,240 and
505 billion pesos.

9.       The urban poor often live in deteriorated, temporary and crowded conditions,
lacking adequate sanitation and water supply. Approximately 9 percent of the population
does not have access to an improved water source, and the lack of sewerage in20 percent of
urban centers i s a serious environmental problem for the country. Although Colombia has
achieved substantial reductions in child mortality,' including child mortality from diarrheal
diseases, the costs associated with diarrheal morbidity from contaminated water and
inadequatehygiene inbothchildren and adults remainhigh.

10.      Air pollution is one of the most widespread and serious problems in Colombia's
cities and industrial corridors. Colombia i s highly urbanized with 72 percent of its
population living in cities. Although air pollution levels are moderate in most cities, the
fact that close to 50 percent of the population lives in cities with more than 100,000
inhabitants creates substantial aggregate health effects, associated mainly with particulate

 Childmortalityi s approximately one-thirdlower inColombia, comparedwith the LAC region, and almost 50 percent
lower than the averagefor lower middle-incomecountries.


                                                          7

matter. This results in health impacts such as aeute respiratory illness, particularly in
children, who are the most vulnerable segment of the population, and death from related
diseases such as pneumonia. The Country Environmental Analysis indicates that the bulk
of the associated cost of urban air pollution is linked to mortality. Approximately 6,000
fatalities per year occur due to outdoor air pollution, while an estimated 1,100 fatalities are
related to exposure to indoor air pollution, associated with use of fuel wood, charcoal and
other solid fuels as primary sources of cooking.

11.      Colombia also faces numerous natural disasters such as floods, droughts and
earthquakes. Inthe last quarter century, the country has experiencedsix major earthquakes,
three volcanic eruptions, three landslides and three avalanches at a significant cost to
humanandphysical capital to the country. According to arecent study, more than 4 million
Colombians were affected by natural disastersbetween 1993 and 2000, at an annual cost of
approximately US$453 million (Echeveny Garzbn, 2002). The poorest and most vulnerable
havepaidthe highest costs for these disastersinterms of damages, deaths and lost assets.

12.      To address these environmental challenges, the government of Colombia i s
introducing broad-based reforms to mainstream environmental management in sectoral
policy, in line with their commitment to meet MillenniumDevelopment Goal 7, namely to
"integrate the principles of sustainable development into country policies and programs and
reverse the loss of environmental resources." Inparticular, the government of Colombia
issued the 2002-2006 National Development Plan (Ley 812, Plan Nacional de Desurrollo
2002-2006: Haciu un Estudo Comuniturio). The NationalDevelopment Plan does not have
a specific chapter on environment; rather it stresses the integration of environmental issues
into national priorities, namely, ensuring democratic security, economic growth,
employment, social equity and public participation.       (See Box 3.)    In addition to the
National Development Plan framework, the government of Colombia has also merged most
of the functions of the Ministry of Economic Development (e.g., housing, water and
sanitation and zoning) with the Ministry of Environment to create the Ministry of
Environment, Housing and Territorial Development (Ministerio de Ambiente, Viviendu y
Desarrollo Territorial, MAVDT), allowing for the integration of principles of sustainable
development in key thematic areas (e.g., water supply and sanitation, air quality, urban
planning).

13.      The Bank is well-positioned to encourage and help Colombia improve the
effectiveness and efficiency of the National Environmental System and integrate the
principles of sustainable development into key sectors.           Since the mid-l990s, the
government and the Bank have been closely collaborating on a variety of environmental
issues, ranging from urban environmental management to biodiversity conservation in
forest ecosystems.      This collaboration has taken various forms, ranging from a
comprehensive Country Environmental Analysis to significant investments in water supply
and sanitation and transport. The government appreciatesthe value of the Bank's continued




                                                 8

support, as expressed in the Letter of Development Policy and in the government's
favorable views expressedinthe Urban EnvironmentalManagement ICR.2

14.      The Colombia Country Assistance Strategy (CAS), approved by the Executive
Directors on January 16, 2003, proposed continued support on environmental management
issues via a Programmatic Sustainable Development Structural Adjustment Loan to the
environmental reform agenda of the Uribe Administration, as outlined in the government's
National Development Plan. The government and the Bank agreed that a Programmatic
Sustainable Development Structural Adjustment Loan (now Programmatic Development
Policy Loan for Sustainable Development) would be the most appropriate tool to provide
reliable financial support for the implementation of a medium-term strategy for achieving
the Millennium Development Goals, particularly Goal 7 which seeks to ensure
environmental sustainability andassociatedTargets9, 10, and 11.




  Report No. 26147, June 26,2003.


                                               9

                             Box 3. Colombia's 2002-2006NationalDevelopmentPlan

   The 2002-2006 National DevelopmentPlan (Plan Nacional de Desarrollo 2002-2006: Hacia un Estado Comunitario,
PND) introduces structural, broad-based reforms aimed at mainstreaming environmental considerations in key sectoral
agendas and improving effectiveness of the National Environmental System (SINA).            Integration of environmental
concerns in economic, sectoral and social policies is needed to achieve cost-effective environmental protection and
sustainable development. Likewise, the PND confirms the government of Colombia's commitment to the Millennium
Development Goals (MDGs), and with that, the role of the SINA in achieving Goal 7, which seeks to ensure
environmental sustainability. The PND identifies environmental sustainability as a foundation for economic growth,
employment generation, and socialequity. The PND emphasizesthe needfor improved natural resource managementas a
fundamental factor for developmentand recognizesthe role of the environment as a source of critical goods and services,
with a particular emphasis on water resourcesmanagement:

        Water Resource Management. The PND identifies the declining state of water quality, combined with inadequate
        water supply and sanitation. The PND focuses upon the need for a national Water law, a significant reform to
        mainstreamenvironmentinthe water sector.
        Mainstreaming environmental tools in productive sectors. The PND sets the goal of developing andimplementing
        measures and instrumentsto prevent, control and reduce atmospheric, water and soil contamination. Large urban
        centers and industrial comdors must adopt comprehensive air quality improvement programs. Revising the
        environmental licensing process to streamline procedures, and introducing Strategic Environmental Assessments
        (SEAS)for critical productive sectors are also included as key programsto reachenvironmental sustainability.
        Integrated watershed management. The PND determines specific targets for carrying out integrated water
        resource management plans for at least 500,000 hectares, including protection of more than 120,000 hectares
        within priority watersheds to improve and increase water supply for human consumption and productive
        processes.
        Conservation and sustainable use of natural resources and biodiversity. The PND promotesconsolidation of the
        National System of Protected Areas, as well as the introduction of instruments to reconcile socioeconomic
        pressures and biodiversity conservation.

    Key sectors targetedfor mainstreaminginclude urbandevelopment,transport and energy, and public services:

        Urban Development. The PND sets forth a national housing policy which assures integration of housing with
        promotion of public mass transit systems, upgrading public spaces, improvingpublic services and zoning, together
        with resettlementof urban residentsinhabiting areas prone to natural disasters. The PND also stresses the urgent
        need to develop and implement integrated waste management plans to address the burgeoning solid waste
        problem.
        Transport and Energy. The PND stresses the need for integrating transport, energy and environmental policies.
        Colombia must address air pollution challenges with measures including shifts in fuels and the improvement of
        fuel quality, as well as measures to prevent and control air pollution. The PND includes the development of a
        national fuel policy aimed at reducing gasoline and diesel subsidies, establishing mass transport systems to
        compensate lower and middle classes for the reduction of these subsidies, setting a real-price-system that
        internalizesrealcosts, and improving refineries to achieveinternational efficiency standards.
        Public Services. The PND encouragesincreasedprivate sector participation in the provision of public services and
        promotion of regional schemes for increasing water and sanitation coverage; use of renewable and non-
        conventional energy sources in off-grid areas, and the need for a new regulatory framework for water and
        sanitation that reduces inefficiencies and includes environmental costs (e.g., water use and water pollution fees)
        into the tariff schemes.
        Prevention and Mitigation of Natural Disasters.       The PND promotes prevention and mitigation of natural
        disastersas part of the planning process at the regional level.

    Finally, the PND aims to improve governance mechanisms, promote decentralization and strengthenthe SINA
through: (i)increasedaccountability and (ii) increasedtransparencyand public participation inthe selectionof regional
environmentalauthorities.


                                                             10

                                11.    COUNTRY CONTEXT

RECENTECONOMICDEVELOPMENTSINCOLOMBIA
15.      Colombia's economy and fiscal balances have improved since the initiation of the
Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) inDecember2002. The near stagnation of the economy
during the four years prior to the CAS has since given way to a robust economic recovery
and strengthening of consumer confidence. While a large part of the strengthening can be
attributed to the much improved global economic environment-world                 growth has
accelerated, the cost of international credit has fallen, and the prices of Colombia's primary
exports have risen-domestic factors, particularly the improved security situation and stable
macroeconomic polices have contributed substantially to the country's recovery.

16.      Improving conditions are reflected by an almost 1 percentage point increase in
private investment as a share of GDPsince 2002 and real economic growth that has doubled
from 1.6 percent in2002 to nearly 4 percentin2003 and 2004. Faster economic growthhas
lowered Colombia's unemployment rate from over 17 percent in 2002 to less than 12
percent in November 2004 (the lowest unemployment rate in the last four years). Fiscal
balances have also improved as the combined public sector deficit decreased from 3.7 of
GDP in 2002 to 2.7 percent in 2003, and it is projected to fall to 1.2 percent in 2004, below
the 2.5 percent target agreed to under the country's current IMF stand-by agreement.
Inflationhas also remained constrained, falling from 7.0 percent in 2002 to 5.5 percent last
year, within the government's statedtarget.

17.      Nonetheless, significant economic challenges and vulnerabilities remain for the
country. Global interests rates are expectedto rise over the coming year as monetary policy
tightens further in Europe and the United States; world economic growth and commodity
prices, particularly oil, begin to moderate, and 2004's large inflows of international capital
begins to stabilize at more modest levels. While positive external factors helped to spur
Colombia's economic growth and improved its overall fiscal balance last year, growth in
public spending-particularly     in pensions and transfers to the regions-is     putting ever-
greater demands on the fiscal accounts which, in turn, are reducing the fiscal space for
much needed investment. These trends are not sustainable, and it i s hoped that pending
legislation to control growing pension liabilities and increase budget flexibility will be
approved and help to slow these pressures.

18.      Fiscal Issues.    In the years prior to the 2002 CAS, new realities emerged in
Colombia's fiscal accounts, including significant deficits, growing public debt, and large
future liabilities. The rapidexpansion of public sector spending throughout the 1990s(from
25 percent of GDP in 1990 to over a 34 in 2003) combined with growing pension and other
liabilities, led to significant and persistent structural deficits. As public spending rapidly
surpassed the government's revenue-generatingcapacity, much of the fiscal deficit became
increasingly financed with debt, both domestic and external. As a result of these deficits,
debt peakedat about 54.2 percentof GDPin2002.

19.      Revenues. Since the 2002 CAS, the Uribe Administrationhas made some important
improvements on the revenue side of the fiscal balance. In December 2002, Law 788 was
approved, a reform supported by the Bank's Fiscal and Institutional Adjustment Loan
(FIAL). This law (i)      eliminated some of the exemptions and expanded the tax base for the
value-added tax; (ii)reduced the wage exemption under the personal income tax and
established a ceiling on it;3and (iii) out the corporate income tax exemption for
                                           phased
capital gains from sales of stock, mutual funds, real estate, and for profits from previously
privileged corporate, contracts, funds, and bonds. Although the tax reform Law 788 of
December 2002 established a framework for tax reform and made significant advances
towards reducing prevailing inefficiencies and distortions in the Colombian tax system, it
also introduced a few its own of distortionary tax measures that were used to meet short-
term revenue targets. This reform brought additional revenues of 0.7 percent of GDP in
2003 and about 1.1 percentof GDP in2004.

20.     Expenditures. On the expenditure side, reforms have been very modest as budget
rigidities and legal challenges to cuts have slowed progress. In 2003, a fiscal referendum
designedto close the deficit by reducing spending was rejected, thereby creating substantial
fiscal gaps in the 2003 and 2004 budgets. On December 29, 2003 Congress approved Law
863-a    tax package designed to fill part of the fiscal gap created by the rejection of the
referendum, but much of these were stopgap measures and fundamental changes are still
needed.

21.     Colombia's budget rigidity i s reflected in two main areas where expenditures have
continued to grow: transfers to subnational governments and to the pensions system. In


  The ceiling includes all forms of compensation and thus reduces incentives for non-taxed compensation to
employees.


                                                       12

2002, Law 715 was approved to control some of the growth rate of transfers to subnational
governments; further reform i s needed if the system i s to be sustainable. Transfers to the
pension system will continue expand over the next decade and must be curtailed if the
government is to keep its obligation to provide a safety net to the poorest elderly citizens.

22.    Colombia has a constitutionally mandated pay-as-you-go pension system managed
through the Znstituto de Seguridud Social (ISS). Although the system underwentreforms in
1993 through Law 100, it is still generating major deficits that need to be covered through
large central government transfers. Law 100 addressed issues of low contributions and
overly generous retirement benefits; however, it did not take into consideration
demographic changes (it calculated an average payout lifetime of 15 years, which has now
reachedclose to 26 years) and did not eliminate a number of very expensive specialpension
regimes. Over the last five years, average contributions to the system have remained
constant at 1.1 percent of GDP, while payouts have increasedfrom 4.4 percent in 2000 to
5.7 percent in 2004. In the absence of an additional reform, the cash-flow deficit of 4.6
percent of GDP in 2004 is projected to continue its upward trend over the next years,
peakingaround 5 percent of GDP in 2013. The net present value of the contingent stock is
about 170percentof GDP.

23.    Inthe second semester of 2004, the government presented a proposed constitutional
amendment to Congress to make the pensions system more sustainable. This proposal
requires eight legislative debates and i s expected to be passed by June 2005. Among the
key elements of this proposal are the following: (i)     introduction of a mandate for financial



                                                  13

sustainability of the pensions system into the Constitution; (ii)           elimination of special
regimes; (iii)ineligibility o f negotiating pensions issues in collective bargaining
agreements; (iv) elimination of the 14th salary (mesudu 14); and (v) cap on individual
pension entitlements of the equivalent of 25 minimum wages. The government estimates
that these measures will reduce the pension system imbalances significantly, leading to a
reduction o f about 20 percent in the net present value of the accumulated operational
deficit.

24.       Despite the increases in transfers and set backs on the reforms in expenditures, the
government has been able to reduce its deficit and its debt stock. The deficit fell from 3.7
percent of GDP in 2002 to a projected 1.2 percent of GDP in 2004, while the net debt stock
has fallen from 54.2 percent of GDP to less than 50 percent of GDP. Although the fiscal
balance and stock o f debt has improved since 2002, vulnerabilities remain. Much of the
recent improvements can be attributed to the favorable external environment, in particular,
to high oil prices and capital inflows that have appreciated the peso substantially and
reduced the size of the foreign currency denominated external debt. Moreover, structural
reform on the expenditure side of the fiscal accounts i s still necessary to move Colombia
toward long-term fiscal sustainability.

MACROECONOMICOUTLOOK AND DEBT SUSTAINABILITY


25.        Macroeconomic Outlook.          As mentioned above, the Government has revised
upwards its projections for economic growth rate to 4.0 percent (from 3.3 percent), and
consumer price inflation to 5.5 percent (from 5 percent). Since prospects for significantly
improving the factors supporting domestic demand (employment,                      internal peace,
investment and credit) are modest, it i s likely that GDP growth will continue to hover at 4.0


                                                     14

percent throughout the next three years. Nevertheless, there are upside and downside risks
inthis scenario that will hinge on the success of fiscal adjustmentefforts; the strengthof the
global economic expansion; and the direction of global interest rates and oil prices.

26.     Industrialproduction will continue to be gearedtowards non-traditional exports, the
prospects of which are marredby uncertainty regarding economic developments in the US
and inVenezuela. Imports are expectedto grow by 5.4 percentin2005, andcombined with
lower exports, are expected to moderately increasethe current account deficit to 2.7 percent
of GDP in 2005. While the same trend is expected to continue in 2006, with a moderate
appreciation of the currency contributing to a current account deficit of 2.9 percent of GDP,
this figure is expected to fall slightly to 2.6 percent of GDP by 2007. FDI flows are
expected to increase due to the expected Free Trade Agreement (FTA) with the United
States, but will likely remain modest due to continued policy uncertainty and a moderate
recovery. Also, given the appreciation of the exchange rate, the positive effects of the free
trade agreement will probably not bereflected inthe forecastedperiod.

27.     Since 2002, when the government initiated the reforms supported by the FIAL
program, the net non-financial public debt to GDP ratio has declined from 54.1 to less than
50 percent as a result of both macroeconomic policies and real exchange rate changes.
Colombia is expected to continue to make headway on the fiscal front. The non- financial
public sector balance is projected to increase slightly from 1.7 percent of GDP in 2004 to 2
percent of GDP in 2007, while the primary balance is expected to remain around 2.7
percent of GDP.

28.     The Government's program calls for increasedexternal financing in 2005, with net
borrowing of about US$1.5 billion. Net disbursements from multilaterals will increase
through 2007, with the bulk of the increase coming from the World Bank. Interest rates
paid on bonds (indomestic currency) are expectedto rise slightly from 13.1 percent 2004 to
14.7 percent in 2007, as global interest rates are expectedto rise.

                            Table 1: Key Economic Indicators




Source: Staff projections.




                                                  15

29.      Debt SustainabilityAnalysis. Colombia's present level of public debt at about 47.5
percent of GDP (down from a peak of 54 percent in 2002) remains relatively high, but
appears manageable in the near term even when considering possible combination of
adverse economic shocks. Many recognize (including the government) that debt as a share
of GDP needs to continue to decline due to its high fiscal cost and the financing
vulnerabilities to which it exposes the county. The government's target i s to reduce the
level of debt to 38 percent of GDP by 2015. This document analyzes the debt prospects in
four different ways:         (i)a baseline projection with historical average values of key
parameters; (ii)    an analysis of policy responses that would be necessary for the government
to meet its debt reduction target in the face of possible economic shocks; (iii)              projections of
what would happen to debt ratios if economic shocks hit, but there i s no fiscal policy
response, and iv) an analysis of the sustainability of total public plus private external debt.
(For a full description of the analytical work i s available form the CMU).

30.      The baseline scenario makes the following key assumptions:

         (i)      Historical average real GDP growth of 3.2 percent, which i s below last
                  year's growth of 3.7 percent,
         (ii)     A primary balance of 2.3 percent. Last year it was 2.9 percent, but this
                  includes one percentage point surplus from subnational governments and
                  public entities, which i s not be readily available to service the debt,
         (iii)    30 percent depreciation of the real exchange rate, bringing it back to its
                  historical average over a five year period beginning in2005,4
         (iv)     An average implicit real interest rate on domestic and external debt of 6.3
                  percent, which i s currently 6.4 percent.

Inthis scenario, the government achieves its target of reducingthe debt ratio to 38 percent
inten years.

31.      The second and third sets of scenarios use three adverse shocks to this baseline-an
immediate depreciation of the real exchange rate of 30 percent in 2005 (instead of spread
over 5 years), a reduction of growth to -2.1 percent for 2005 (two standard deviations from
the average) and then gradually returning to its average by 2009, and a rise o f the real
interest rate to 8.6 percent in 2005 and returning gradually to 6.3 percent by 2009. And
there i s the combination shock, where all three adverse shocks occur. If the government
still wants to meet its target debt ratio for 2015, it will have to tighten fiscal policy from the
baseline primary balance of 2.3 percent, up to 3.7 percent in the case o f the combination
shock. (See Table 2.)




  Since about half of Colombia's debt i s foreign, mostly in US$, the exchange rate has a large and
instantaneousimpact on the debt ratio. In the decline of the debt ratio from its peak of 54 percent of GDP in
2002 to 47.5 percent at the end of 2004, the 30 percent real appreciation of the exchange rate accounts for six
of the eight percentagepoint decline. The exchange rate is unlikely to retain all of this recent appreciation, so
the government should continue with fiscal caution, despite decline o f the debt ratio.

32.       Ifthese shocks came andpolicy stayedpassive, with primarybalancesunchangedat
2.3 percent, then debt ratios would jump initially and then decline, but the government
would not achieve its original target for reducing the debt ratio. With each of the individual
shocks, the debt ratio would be below 45 percent by 2015. With the combination of all
three shocks, the ratio would peak at about 62 percent of GDP in 2007, and then slowly fall
to about 57 percent in2015. This level of debt i s certainly high and would put considerable
fiscal pressure on the government, but it is not explosive and could be contained by
maintaining a primary balance of 2.3 percent of GDP, which is within the historical
experienceof Colombia. (See Figure4.)

33.     Analyzing the sustainability of the external debt requires a model that incorporates
another set of key macroeconomic variables such as Colombia's current account and allows
us to model another set of different shocks, in particular, one for international capital flows
into the country. Total external debt service (public and private) as a percent of exports o f
goods, services, and income, while having fallen from a higho f above 67 percent in2002 to
more manageable levels of less than 40 percent now, remains one of the highest in Latin
America (justbelow Argentina's 42 percent).

34.     Nonetheless, Colombia's total external debt profile (public and private) i s likely to
be sustainable. Total external debt i s expected to decline to 36.1 percent o f GDP under the
Bank's baseline scenario. The primary risk associated with external debt sustainability is
exchange rate risk. A depreciation of the real exchange rate in excess o f 30 percent within
one year would increase the total external debt to GDP ratio from about 42 percent
currently, to over 50 percent, generating a significant risk of market closure in the near
term. A two-standard deviation drop in international capital inflows from the historical
average would generate an increase in the external debt to GDP ratio to about 46 percent,
but this is unlikely to generate a debt crisis. Total external debt reached nearly 48 percent
in2001 without a crisis inan economic environment that was less favorable than today. A
transitory peak of 46 percent total external debt would put pressure on financing, but i s
expected to be manageable.

                        111.   THE GOVERNMENT'SPROGRAM

A.       IMPROVINGEFFECTIVENESSAND EFFICIENCYOFTHE NATIONAL
         ENVIRONMENTALSYSTEM

1. StrategicPlanning andMonitoring Frameworkfor SustainableDevelopment

35.      For thirty years, Colombia has undertaken a number of policy initiatives and
strengthened its institutional capacity to ensure the protection and management of natural
resources. The first integrated legislation for natural resources in Latin America was
establishedin Colombia in 1973. The Constitution of 1991and Law 99 of 1993 defined the
consolidation of the National Environmental System (SINA), which constitutes one of the
stronger institutional frameworks for environmental management in Latin America. It i s
presided by MAVDT, together with a group of 33 Regional Autonomous Corporations-
together with urban environmental authorities, which are largely responsible for addressing
urban air quality andwater quality issues-and       four researchinstitutes. (See Box 1above.)

36.      InColombia, public sector spendingonthe environment-including              spendingby the
CARs, the Ministry of Environment (precursor to MAVDT) and research institutes-
averaged 0.38 percent of GDP between 1995 and 200Z5 While totaE spending increased
slightly during the second half of the 1990s, this masks significant changes in levels of
spendingby the national government versus the CARs: total central government spending
on the environment declined while spendingby the CARs increased.

37.      Between 1995 and 2002, 71 percent of SINA funding for operational expenditures
and 85 percent of SINA funding for investments was allocated to the CARs. Seventy-eight
percent of the funding for operational expenditures and 86 percent of funding for
investments was self-generated.       CARs use a variety of mechanisms to raise revenue,
including property taxes, electricity taxes, effluent fees, compensatory fees, water use fees,
licenses and permits, fines and sanctions, the sale of goods and services, profits on
investments, and inter-institutional agreements. Of these sources, property taxes account
for approximately 35 percent of total revenues, profits on investments account for 34
percent,6electricity taxes account for 10percent, inter-institutional agreements and the sale

38.      Despite institutional advancements with the creation and consolidation of SINA,
Colombia faces significant challenges related to land, air and water degradation, compromising
its potential for sustainable economic development. As noted above, an assessment of the cost of
environmental degradation in Colombia found that the country's most significant problems are
associated with air and water pollution, inadequate water supply and sanitation, and hygiene.*
The source of these problems can be summarized by (i)             poor urban air quality; (ii)    inadequate
drinking water supply, sanitation facilities and hygiene; (iii) disasters; and (iv) use of
                                                                       natural
solid fuels in rural dwellings, causing poor indoor air quality. That said, over the past decade,
SINA has spent approximately 3 billion Colombian pesos without real and measurable impact
indicators to promote linkages between national and regional priorities, nor to increase
understanding of the impacts of key sectors on the environment.

GovernmentActions

39.      The transformation of the Ministry of Environment to the Ministry of Environment,
Housing and Regional Development (MAVDT) opens up significant opportunities for
mainstreaming environment into key sectors, such as urban development, water supply and
sanitation, and transport. However, as indicated above, Colombia faces an urgent need for (i)              a
national strategic planning framework to promote integration of environmental concerns in
economic, sectoral and social policies, and (ii)planning instruments through which the
government can articulate and harmonize national and regional priorities with the above-
mentioned policy framework. These actions support the reforms set forth in the 2002-2006
National Development Plan and confirm the government's commitment to achieving the
MillenniumDevelopment Goals.

40.      The guidelines for aNational SustainableDevelopment Policy, approved bythe CONPES
on March 14, 2005, serves as the umbrella document for the SusDevDPL as well as the
complementary Sustainable Development Investment project.' Based upon the analytical work
carried out under the Country Environmental Analysis, together with consultant reports prepared
in conjunction with the SusDevDPL, the policy document puts into motion a series of activities
andinvestmentsto improve environmental governance and urban environmental management.

41.      Presidential Decree 1200 of 2004 represents a significant step forward in improving
performance of the CARS and supporting the achievement of MDG targets relating to
environmental sustainability; likewise, it enhances public participation and transparency in
decision-making. The decree, approved in 2004, establishes a system of performance indicators
to measure impact and results of environmental investments at the regional level via ten-year

Regional Environmental Management Plans (Planes de Gestidn Ambiental Regional, PGARs)
and Three-year Action Plans (Plan de Accidn Trienal, PATs), both of which each CAR is

42.     The Three-year Action Plan, prepared in consultation with stakeholders including
Regional Autonomous Corporations, municipalities, and civil society representatives, details the
Corporation's short-term strategy for achieving the goals set forth in the PGAR. Six sustainable
development objectives together with fifteen impact indicators are included in Decree 1200 of
2004 to evaluate environmental management by the CARs.            These indicators cover issues
including deforestation rates and forest conservation efforts, development of green markets,
rationalization and optimization of consumption of renewable natural resources, reduction in
health impacts associated with environmental factors, and reduction in vulnerability risk
associated with natural disasters, amongst others. These objectives and their corresponding
indicators serve to promote a better linkage between national and regional priorities and to
strengthenthe focus on results and impacts linkedto the environment, rather than on institutional
processes. Each year, MAVDT will review progress towards achieving the goals set forth in the
PATs and PGARs. CARs which do no meet the goals set forth in their own PATs are at risk of
having their Directors removedby the Boardof Directors of CAR.

2. Improved inter-institutional coordination and increased public participation in
environmentaldecision-making

43.     Law 99 of 1993 created Colombia's Ministry of the Environment (now MAVDT) and
established the National Environmental System (SINA) around 14 guiding principles.                As
indicated inthe description of the National Environmental System (see Box l), SINA consists
                                                                                  the
of numerous actors, coordination and planning mechanisms, legal norms, mechanisms for
implementingand enforcing policy, financial resources, and mechanisms for public participation.
In addition to MAVDT and the CARs, the SINA includes representation from the public and
private sectors, regional authorities, other governmental institutions, research institutes, and civil
society organizations. Two of the guiding principles were targeted at improving design of
investment projects. These include (i)    environmental protection as a coordinated task between
the state, community, NGOs, and the private sector; and (ii)   EIAsto serve as abasis for decision-
making with respect to activities that may significantly affect the environment. Civil society i s
involvedthrough the participation of associations from productive sectors, environmental NGOs,
and indigenous andblack communities. At the regional level, participation inthe CARs includes
representatives from municipal and regional government authorities, private sector associations,
NGOs, indigenous peoples and Afro-Colombian communities. Urban environmental authorities
are largely responsible for issues related to air quality, water quality, and reduction in
vulnerability to natural disasters.

44.     Likewise, Law 99 created the Technical Advisory Council (Consejo Ticnico Asesor,
CTA), headedby the Vice-Minister of the Environment, to advise the Minister on the design of
environmental policy and regulations, as well as on initiatives of national interest.
Representation on the CTA includes MAVDT, public and private universities, and


                                                   25

representativesof the agricultural, mining, petroleum, and manufacturing sectors. Furthermore,
Law 99 defined the system for issuing environmental licenses. A well-developed system for
users to request, pay and follow-up on projects subject to environmental licenses is in place;
natural resource user permits have been incorporated into environmental licenses to reduce
procedures and time; and joint work with different sectors, particularly mining and energy, has
been conducted to streamlineprocedures.

45.      Despite these intentions, the participation of the most vulnerable groups, those who are
directly affected by environmental degradation, has often been inadequate.                          The Country
Environmental Analysis carried out by the Bank has shown that more transparency and
accountability is required as a result of significant regulatory capture and rent-seeking at the
regional and national levels.

GovernmentActions

46.      To promote increased transparency, improved governance, and broader inter-sectoral
coordination, MAVDT and the World Bank have carried out analytical work to identify
necessary reforms inthese areas, including:

         e   Conducting a study to identify mechanisms through which civil society, NGOs, and
             different stakeholdershave identifiedpriorities inColombia from 1973to 2004;'' and

         0   Evaluation and recommendations to enhance the effectiveness, efficiency, and equity
             of the EL4process andenvironmental licensing systeminColombia."

47.      InlinewiththerecommendationcomingfromthesestudiesandotherswithintheCountry
Environmental Analysis, the government of Colombia has initiated a process for improving
environmental governance. Presidential Decree 3345 of 2005 represents a significant step in
improving procedures for the election of CAR Directors, providing more transparency to the
SINA by enhancingpublic participation and transparency. Decree 3345 defines the guidelines to
be followed for the election processes, requiring the Board of Directors of every CAR to assess
the profile, academic trainingand professionalexperience of candidates for the Directorposition.
Likewise, public audiences will allow for civil society input into the selection process, to ensure
a broadconsensus on the candidate selected.

48.      In line with recommendations made in the "Assessment of Colombia's National
Environmental System (SINA)" review carried out as part of the Country Environmental
Analysis (see paras. 87-88 below), Resolution 0449 of 2004 supports inter-sectoral coordination
through the establishment of the SINA Working Group (Grupo SINA) as an institutional
mechanism for periodic discussion of the congruence between national and regional priorities,
continuous consultation and organizational learning, assessment of effectiveness of CARSin

49.     To support implementation of Law 99, Decree 707 of 2005 defines the roles and
responsibilities of the Technical Advisory Council (Consejo TkcnicoAsesor, CTA) to improve
transparency, increase cross-sectoral coordination and ensure that the most vulnerable
communities have a voice inenvironmental decision-making. The decree sets out the procedures

Council members, and establishes issue-based working groups conformed by a broad cross-
section of society (e.g., universities, user groups, private sector, civil society, indigenous
communities, Afro-Colombian communities, non-governmental organizations) to advise the
Council with respect to specific issues.

50.     Decree 1220 of 2005 reforms the environmental licensing system inColombia inorder to
(i)minimize administrative discretionality by government officials and establish clear "rules of
the game" with respect to environmental licensing, (ii) to improve transparency in the licensing
process, and (iii)   simplify and enhance the integration of environmental considerations into
investment projects.    In line with Article 63 of Law 99, and in particular the subsidiary
principle-that   is, requirements adopted by lower levels of government cannot be weaker than
nor weaken those of higher levels of government-the    decree indicates that while the CARs may
issue environmental licenses on relatively smaller projects (e.g., energy projects between 10MW
and 100 MW, construction of secondary roads, small mining projects), only MAVDT is
permitted to issue licenses for larger projects (e.g., energy projects over 100 MW, highway
construction, large miningprojects). Similarly, the decree clarifies procedures for Environmental
Impact Assessments and the process for obtaining environmental licenses, and defines steps for
monitoring and evaluation of licenses and environmental managementplans.
