B.     INTEGRATINGPRINCIPLESOFSUSTAINABLEDEVELOPMENTINTOKEY
       SECTORS
1. IntegratedManagement of WaterResources

51.    Colombia i s a country with abundant water resources.           However, as a result of
deforestation and unequal geographic distribution of water resources, there i s already a severe
problem related to water availability and demand in some areas of the country. According to the
Colombian Institute of Hydrology, Meteorology and Environmental Studies (Instituto de



                                                  28

Hidrologi'a, Meteorologi'a y Estudios Ambientales, IDEAM), more than 14 million inhabitants
could suffer future water shortages.12 Colombia also experiences a large number of natural
disasters related to flooding and drought. Lack of adequate drainage in most urban areas i s
becoming an important factor of urbanflooding.

52.      Likewise, surface and groundwater are not only overexploited but also contaminated,
affecting water quality in rivers, lakes and aquifers. Water quality has deteriorated and in many
cases i s not suitable for many needs; and less than eight percent of discharges are treated.
Studies carried out by the former Ministry of Health (now Ministry of Social Protection) indicate
that more than two million Colombians suffer from water borne diseases derived from deficient
water and sewerage systems. Although child mortality is approximately one-third lower in
Colombia than the Latin American average, morbidity and mortality related to diarrheal diseases
causedby untreateddrinkingwater and poor hygiene are major health problems.

53.      During the 1990s, Colombia had considerable success in expanding water supply and
sanitation coverage, together with an improvement in service quality. According to government
statistics, Colombia has potable water coverage ranging from 96 percent of inhabitants in major
cities to 82 percent in small municipalities; similarly, sanitation networks reach 88 percent of
households in major cities and 60 percent in small municipalities, respectively. Continuity of
water supply increased from 19.5 hourdday in 1995 to 21.6 in 2000. However, the government
aims to increase coverage of potable water while addressing a growing environmental problem
related to sewage in urban centers and rural areas. In particular, this will require public and
private sector companies to improve efficiency and environmental standards regarding water
quality and wastewater treatment.

54.      Contributing to the deterioration of surface and groundwater resources is a burgeoning
solid waste problem. Numerous studies indicate the close links between improper solid waste
and hazardous waste management with declining water quality.                     According to the National
Superintendent of Public Services, daily generation of solid waste in Colombia i s about 27,500
tons. There are more than 700 open garbage dumps located in medium-sized and smaller
municipalities, where public service for waste collection and disposal i s deficient. Very few of
the nation's registeredlandfills operate ina satisfactory manner, whereas inthe majority of cases,
uncontrolled landfills threaten surface and groundwater in surrounding areas. Lack of clarity
regarding minimumtechnical specifications as well as deficient inter-institutional mechanisms
for adequate closure of open dumps and construction of new landfills, has resulted in weak
compliance andenforcement.

55.      To limit degradationof water resources, Decree 1713 of 2002 established integrated solid
waste management plans, a long-term planning instrument to include socioeconomic,
environmental, institutional, technical and operational information required to achieve defined
goals, and investments to be carried out during that period of time. A two-year compliance
period was given to municipalities to develop and obtain approval from city councils for these
goals and investments. However, lack of resources, institutional and technical capacity, as well
as new election of mayors in 2003, ledthe central government to issue Decrees 1140and 1505 in
2003 to establisha new timetable basedon municipal size. Municipalities have the obligation of
incorporating land use plans into solid waste management strategies that, considering population

'*IDEAM,   2004, Profile of the State of Natural Resources and the Environment inColombia.


                                                            29

growth and urban expansion, must include disposal sites approved by environmental authorities.
Many departments currently face a situation in which they lack even a single approved site for
disposal of solid wastes.

GovernmentActions

56.     To address Colombia's growing problem related to declining water quality, the
government has prepared and presented for Congressional approval a national water law. The
law addresses issues relating to (i) water quality and control of point and non-point sources of
pollution; (ii)  regulations regarding management of water run-off and urban drainage; (iii)
allocation of water rights; (iv) reduction of vulnerability to natural disasters; (v) management of
marine and coastal resources; and (vi) conservation of important water ecosystems.
Strengthening performance in the water sector requires improving existing regulations to control
water pollution, including introduction of economic instruments to improve efficiency and
equity; and water quality standards for human consumption, recreational uses and irrigation.
Likewise, this will require strategies for the formulation, socialization and implementation of
programs for accountability, transparency and governance to promote compliance with water
quality standards. Finally, Colombia must develop planning instruments to design and build
regional waste managementdisposal sites, and establish economic andfinancial instruments such
as compensation fees to municipalities that allow site construction for waste disposal.

57.     From an institutionalperspective, a variety of uncoordinated efforts havebeencarried out
inrecent decades with respectto integrated water resourcemanagement; potable water andbasic
sanitation services have been managed by various ministries (e.g., public works, health,
economic development), while water resources management has been a particular responsibility
of environmental authorities. As a result, dispersed investments from states, municipalities, and
environmental authorities have been made in reforestation and water basin management with
little correlation to water supply needs, and significant investments have been made in waste
water treatment with little or no impacts in improvingwater quality.

58.     The government of Colombia has implemented important institutional changes through
the creation of the Ministry of Environment, Housing and Regional Development (MAVDT).
Furthermore, as part of these changes, the Institute of Hydrology, Meteorology and
Environmental Studies (IDEAM) is reorienting its priorities towards providing required data for
decision-making and investments. Important advancements have been reached in developing
water scarcity indicators as an input for reforestation investments, and water quality monitoring
accreditation protocols.

59.     To address the above-mentioned concerns, Colombia is engagedin a fundamental reform
of the water sector through the formulation of a national Water Resources Management Law.
Buildingupon international best practice in the water sector, the draft law defines institutional
responsibilities (e.g., National Water Council, which includes participation from numerous
governmental institutions); planning instruments (e.g., twenty-year National Water Plan,
watershed management plans); priorities in the assignment of water rights and water use; length
of water concessions; means for controlling water pollution; rights and responsibilities over the
use of groundwater resources; protection of natural ecosystems, and in particular coastal and
marine resources; use of economic and fiscal instruments to support integrated water resources
management (e.g., "polluter pays" principal, user fees); and means for promoting public


                                                   30

participation in decisions regarding the water sector.      The draft Law builds on extensive
analytical work carried out during the Country Environmental Analysis and project preparation
(see Table 6 below), including the use of a seven-person team of international consultants to
review institutional aspects of water management, allocation of uses, control of contamination,
and urban drainage; as well as a study tour of government officials to Brazil to discuss the
manner in which the Braziliangovernment i s promoting integrated water resourcesmanagement.
Finally, the draft Law has been subject to significant consultations at the national and regional
levels carried out over a year-long period. It is expected that the draft Law will be approved in
2006.

60.     The government of Colombia has recently negotiated investments with the World Bank
and IDB which would assist in the achievement of important National Development Plan (Plan
Nacional de Desarrollo 2002-2006: Hacia un Estado Comunitario, PND) goals: the new World
Bank-financed project will support compliance with PND goals relating to water supply and
sanitation; and the new IDB-financed project will increase resources for watershed management
and reforestation, so as to achieve goals related to improving water availability. For the first
time, data provided by IDEAM will be fundamental in targeting allocation of investments at the
river basin level.

61.      To support implementation and enforcement of national policies and decrees relating to
solid waste management, Presidential Decrees 1140 and 1505 of 2003 establish new deadlines
for municipalities to formulate and receive city council approval of the Integrated Solid Waste
Management Plans. Compliance dates have been set based upon the size of municipalities, to
maximize coverage basedupon population size. Likewise, as part of the fiscal reformpackageof
December 2002, the government of Colombia included Article 58 that establishes that any
project included within an Integrated Solid Waste Management Plan will obtain tax exemption as
an environmental investment. This i s the first financial instrument developed for future
implementation of suchplans.

62.     Finally, through Presidential Decree 1200 of 2004 (see paragraph 41 above), the
government has established a monitoring and evaluation system to measure the efficiency and
effectiveness of the CARS,including indicators aimed at mainstreaming environment into water
and sanitation, such as conservation and reforestation of areas linked with water supply to
improve availability, and the impact of waste water treatment investments on downstream
communities.

Actions to be supported under the Proposed Operation

        a.     Presentation of draft Water Resources Management Law to Congress, setting
               requirements on (i)  allocation of water rights; (ii)control of water pollution and
               quality; (iii)
                            regulations on management of water run-off and urbandrainage; (iv)
               reduction of vulnerability to natural disasters; (v) management of marine and
               coastal resources; and (vi) conservation of important water ecosystems.

        b.     Issuance of Presidential Decree 838 of 2005 to promote and facilitate the
               planning, construction and operation of sanitary landfills, as well as to establish
               norms for landfill operation.



                                                   31

Actions to be supported in subsequent operations include:

        a.      Congressional approval of a new Water ResourcesManagement Law.

        b.      Implementation of an Action Plan to improve laboratory accreditation and
                dissemination of water quality monitoring protocols.

        c.      Implementation of a technical assistance program to close open dumps in at least
                75 municipalities.

        d.      Issuance of decrees related to implementation of the Water Resources
                Management Law, including water quality standards and wastewater discharge
                standards.

        e.      Design and approval by CONPES of a national policy relating to control of water
                pollution, including an economic instruments scheme (such as pollution charges)
                and quality standards.

        f.      Formulation, socialization and implementation of programs for accountability,
                transparency and governanceto promote compliance with water quality standards.

        g.      Adapt Water Unit in MAVDT in line with new Water Resources Management
                Law, to ensure appropriate institutional framework to support implementation of
                the law.

                to the following priority themes: (i)
                                                    revision of schemes for user compliance; (ii)
                permissible discharge limits; and (iii)a new tariff methodology for use of
                pollutioncharges for liquiddischargesto surface and ground water bodies.


2. Improving UrbanAir Quality

63.     There is substantial research evidence from around the world that outdoor urban air
pollution has significant negative impacts on public health and results in premature deaths,
chronic bronchitis, and respiratory disorders. The air pollutant that has shown the strongest
associationwith these health endpoints i s particulate matter (PM), and especially particulates of
less than 10 microns in diameter (PM 10) or smaller. International research on environmental
health provides strong evidence that it is exposure to particulate matter of 2.5 microns (PM 2.5)
that has the most significant health'effects; closely linked with high incidences in respiratory
diseases. Gaseous pollutants (ozone, CO, SO*, and NOx) are generally not thought to be as
damaging as fine particulates.

64.     Colombia i s highly urbanized with approximately 50 percent of the nation's population
living in cities with more than 100,000 inhabitants, and 30 percent live in cities with more than
one million inhabitants. Few cities in Colombia collect air quality data, and none presently
collect data on PM 2.5. Some areas of BogotQexperience a high frequency of days exceeding
established air quality norms. Among the principal sources of particulate matter are vehicles,



                                                   32

especially diesel-run vehicles for public transportation. Increaseddata collection i s necessary to
improve decision-making indefiningstandards, especially relatedto particulate matter.

65.     Although Colombia has a long tradition in developing regulatory actions for air pollution
control, national governmental action regarding air pollutionprevention and control over the past
decade has been limited. The first air quality monitoring networks were installed in the main
urban centers back in 1967.         In 1979, Congress approved air quality regulations while
environmental standards were established in 1982. However, over the past decade, only one
significant decree- Decree948/1995 -was approved. As a result, the regulations developed and
approved in 1982 are still in place. At the local level, with the exception of BogotB, where in
2003 the urban environmental authority approved emission standards for mobile and stationary
sources more stringent than national standards, regulatory activity has also beenlimited.

66.      Under the decentralized National Environmental System (SINA), MAVDT is responsible
for enacting national regulations for air pollutioncontrolbasedon technical informationprovided
by IDEAM; regional environmental authorities have to enforce these standards. Nevertheless,
most of the environmental authorities have neither the technical capability nor the resources to
exercise their responsibilities (Le., planning, implementation, enforcement and monitoring).
Fifteen environmental authorities operate air quality monitoring networks; however, there has not
been a thorough evaluation about the real monitoring equipment needs in cities and industrial
corridors, and most air quality networks do not have sufficient resources to cover maintenance
costs.

GovernmentActions

67.      The National Development Plan (Plan Nacional de Desarrollo 2002-2006: Hucia un
Estado Comunitario, PND) stresses the need for integrating transport, energy and environmental
policies. This requires measures such as improving fuel quality and substitution for cleaner
fuels, as well as measures to prevent and control atmospheric pollution. The PND includes the
development of a national fuel policy aimed at reducing gasoline and diesel subsidies,
establishing mass transport systems to compensate lower and middle classes for the reduction of
these subsidies, setting a price system that internalizes real costs, as well as upgrading fuel
refineries to achieveinternational efficiency standards.

68.      Although there i s no national coordinated strategy aimed at preventing and controllingair
pollution, different sectors of the economy are undertaking important actions. The energy sector
included within the National Energy Plan (i)     significant proposals aimed at the optimization of
the refineries inBarrancabermejaand Cartagena to achieve international efficiency standards, (ii)
promotion of the use of natural gas for vehicles, and (iii)    development of new and alternative
clean fuels, such as biodiesel and alcohol fuels. In addition to promoting cleaner fuels, the
transport sector is promoting an expansion of the mass transit systems, with support from the
World Bank.

69.      MAVDT has supported these initiatives, developing a multisectoral agenda with
governmental agencies to mainstream environment, particularly air quality issues and their
impact on environmental health in key sectors. Recognizing positive externalities through the
Clean Development Mechanism of the Kyoto Protocol, MAVDT, IDEAM, and other SINA
institutions have been active in promoting clean energy generation, energy efficiency, public


                                                    33

transportation, and development of accreditation protocols for air quality network operations and
data analysis.

70.     The above-mentioned actions are critical for Colombia to meet MDGtargets, and comply
with the 2002-2006 National Development Plan. Likewise, these actions respond to needs
identified in the Country Environmental Analysis, in which the most vulnerable populations
identified poor air quality as the leading environmental concern in Colombia. The proposed
SusDevDPL supports the development of (a) a comprehensive National Policy for Air Pollution
Prevention and Control which sets forth guidelines for developing a national air policy, which
emphasizes (i)  measurement of key pollutants that are associatedwith significant health impacts,
(ii)research to better understand the causal links between exposure to pollutants and health
impacts, and promotion of measures to improve air quality and mitigate these health impacts in
urban and rural areas (e.g., improving fuel quality, removing pricing subsidies for diesel fuels,
upgrading refineries, improving emissions standards); and (b) a national policy and action plan
related to integrated urban environmental management, including (i)      an evaluation of existing
mass transit systems, (ii) promotion of new public transportation systems (i.e., municipalities
lacking such systems), (iii)  land use planning to reduce urban sprawl and re-densification of
municipalities to improve delivery of urban services, and (iv) reduction of risks related to natural
disasters.  Furthermore, a complementary Sustainable Development Investment project will
upgrade the national air quality monitoring system, support the development of a Strategic
Environmental Assessment related to fuel quality, and provide technical assistance and
equipment to strengthen MAVDT and DNP, together with the CARS,with respect to air quality
issues.

Actions to be supported under the Proposed Operation

        a.     Design and approval by the CONPES of a national action plan related to air
               pollution control, which emphasizes (i)  measurement of key pollutants that are
               associated with significant health impacts (such as P M 2.5); (ii)researchto better
               understand the causal links between health impacts (such as cardiopulmonary
               diseases, lung cancer) and pollutants; and (iii)promotion of measures to mitigate
               these health impacts. (CONPES 3344)

        b.     Design and approval by CONPES of a national integrated urban environmental
               policy. (CONPES 3305)

71.     Actions to be supportedin subsequent operations include:

        a.     Design and approval by the CONPES of a national air pollution control policy,
               based in part on information obtained through air quality monitoring conducted
               underthe SustainableDevelopment Investment project.

        b.     Issuance of new decrees to establish and implement a new air pollution control
               scheme to promote behavioral change inline with reduction of urban air pollution.

        c.     Implementation of a national air quality monitoring system to monitor P M 2.5,
               PM 10, and ozone inpriority urban areas.




                                                   34

                      Box 4. ColombiaSustainableDevelopmentInvestmentProject

    The proposed Sustainable Development Investment Project, to be presented to the Board in early FY06,
will finance targeted actions and investments to implement policies and reforms that are supported under the
Sustainable Development DPL Program. The investment project's development objective is to alleviate the
effects of environmental degradation on the most vulnerable segments of the population, particularly children
who are typically the most affected by this degradation, through improvements in public health, productivity
and quality of life. These improvements will be achieved through reductions in air and water pollution, as well
through improved hygiene and urban environmental management.                 To this end, the objectives of the
Investment Loan are to:
   i. supporttheincorporationofenvironmentalandsocialconsiderations inthepoliciesandprogramsof
         the sectors that principally result in a high cost of environmental degradation, namely water, transport,
          health, and energy; and
  ii. strengthen the planning, monitoring, and oversight of national, regional and localenvironmental
         management and policies, by -
             a.   strengthening the capacity of the Ministry of Environment, Housing and Territorial
                  Development (MAVDT) to improve the effectiveness and efficiency of its National
                  Environmental Management System (SINA)as well as to implement policies; and
             b. supporting the institutional strengthening of the National Department of Planning (DNP) to
                  design policies that address environmental priorities.

     Specifically, the proposed investment project will finance actions and investments to support the
development and implementation of the DPL Program's policy reforms.                  For example, to support the
development of a national Air Pollution Control Policy, the Investment Project will finance improvements in
the current air quality monitoring networks in Colombia's largest cities, targeted at improving the monitoring
of those particulates that are most harmful (e.g., P M 2.5). Inaddition, analytical studies will also be conducted
to update air quality regulations, evaluate air pollution health risks, and assess fuel quality. Second, the project
will also support the definition and implementation of both the Water Resources Management Law and the
Water Pollution Control Policy, through socialization of the draft Water Law with civil society, the
development and implementation of a national handwashing program to prevent hygiene-related diseases, and
through the updating of regulations, such as those related to wastewater discharges and pollution taxes.
Similarly, the project will support a strengthening and accreditation process for regional laboratories
responsible for monitoring water quality. Third, the recently-issued Decree on the Final Disposal of Solid
Waste will be backed by associated regulations developed under the project, taking into consideration legal,
economic and financial instruments. Furthermore, technical assistance will be provided to 200 municipalities
to support efforts to reduce illegal dumping of solids wastes.
     To improve the effectiveness and efficiency of the SINA, the proposed investment project will provide (i)
actions and investments to strengthen coordination among key stakeholders, includingthe strengthening of the
technical and analytical capacity of MAVDT to implement policies, as well as to improve MAVDTs
coordination with other SINA actors and oversight of CAR activities, and (ii)actions and investments to
support the monitoring of indicators established in the CARs' Three-Year Action plans.                 One of the
institutional strengthening needs-highlighted      in the analytical work carried out in preparation for both the
DPL and CEA-is       for the development o f a well-functioning information system that will enable MAVDT to
monitor and evaluate CAR activities (Le., investments, policy implementation, and regulatory enforcement
activities). As such, the project will support the development of an information system to increase MAVDT's
knowledge more generally of environmental quality at the regional level.             This information will enable
MAVDT to enhance coordination with the CARs, and be used for disseminating environmental data more
broadly to the public; likewise, the information will serve to create a baseline for monitoring progress on the
SusDevDPL Program. Finally, MAVDT will conduct two policy-based SEASon environmental health and
fuel quality under the Investment Project, with a view to reducing and preventing health risks from
environmental factors and identifying responsibility agreements among environmental authorities and other
stakeholders.

            IV.     BANKSUPPORT TO THE GOVERNMENT'S STRATEGY

LINKTO COUNTRYASSISTANCESTRATEGY
72.    The Bank's Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) for Colombia was discussedby the Board
of Executive Directors on January 16, 2003. To support the government's efforts to alleviate
poverty and help Colombia meet the targets for the MDGs, the Bank's strategy supports the
following goals: (i)achieving fast and sustainable growth; (ii)
                                                              sharing the fruits of growth; and
(iii) efficient,accountableandtransparentgovernance.
    building

73.    The proposed project takes a pragmatic approach to supporting the government's efforts
to achieve the MDGs through: (i)   improving the effectiveness and efficiency of the National
Environmental System (SINA); and (ii)   integrating principles of sustainable development into
key sectors, with a particular emphasis on protecting the most vulnerable groups.          The
SusDevDPL directly contributes to the following CAS objectives:

       0  Achieving; Fast and Sustainable Growth: Putting the economy back on a rapid,
          sustainable growth path requires (i)exploiting the synergies between economic
          growth and environmental management, and (ii)strengthening institutional and
          regulatory arrangements for provision of basic services. The SusDevDPL supports
          policies that mainstream environmental considerations in key sectors, improve
          coordination and strengthen consensus among key stakeholders, while supporting the
          development of appropriatefiscal instruments in support of environmental goals.


       0  Buildinp Efficient, Accountable and Transparent Governance: Numerous studies
          have documented high levels of regulatory capture in the Colombian government,
          including within the SINA. The SusDevDPL supports actions which (i)         strengthen
          the SINA with respect to environmental decision-making and policy implementation,
          (ii) improve the collection, management and use of environmental data to assist in
          prioritizing actions and evaluating investment efforts at the national and regional
          levels, (iii)improve coordination between MAVDT, DNP, and CARS, and (iv)
          enhancepublic participation indecision-making.


COLLABORATIONWITHTHE IMFAND OTHER DONORS
74.    The Bank has collaborated with numerous external assistance agencies during project
preparation, including the Andean Development Corporation (Corporacio'nAndina de Fomento,
CAF), the Inter-American Development Bank (IDB), the Dutch International Cooperation
Agency, and the United Nations Environment Programme (UNEP). As part of the preparation of
the proposed project, representatives from CAF, IDB, and UNEP participated in a regional
workshop organized by the Bank on Strategic Environmental Assessment (SEA) in Cartagena,
Colombia in September 2003. The workshop represented an important step in advancing the use
of SEA inthe region to incorporate environmental considerations inkey development policies.

75.    Inaddition, the Bank has closely coordinated with the LDB inthe sharing of documents
and joint missions. In this respect, the proposed operation i s designed to complement IDB's
Urban Social Housing Project as well as IDB's Program to Support the National Environmental


                                                 36

System (SINA    n),both   of which were approved inFY04. The Bank has built synergies between
the proposed SusDevDPL and IDB-financed SINA 11, which aims to support sustainable
development by strengthening national and regional environmental institutions and financing
environmental investments. Basedonjoint World Bank-IDB discussions with the government, it
was agreed that the IDB project would focus specifically on reforestation linked to watershed
management and protected areas, as well as cleaner production for the manufacturing sector, two
areas that are not included under the SusDevDPL. Likewise, IDB technical specialists in the
areas of energy and finance have actively participated in Bank preparation missions for the
SusDevDPL.

RELATIONSHIPTO OTHERBANKOPERATIONS
76.     Inlight of the highprioritybeingattachedby the government to promote competitiveness
of the Colombian productive sector and improving access to basic public infrastructure services
to the least privilegedpopulation segments, the Bank is supporting efforts to establish efficient,
effective, and well-managed infrastructure services. This is due inpart to empiricalevidence of a
strong causal link between the availability of adequate infrastructure services and enabling
economic growth and competitiveness. Moreover, the lack of basic water and sanitation
services, shelter, and secondary and community roads has severe adverse social effects on the
poorest population.

77.     The Bank has a significant investment program related to urban environmental
management. This includes:

    0   Bogota Urban Services Project. The US$100 million loanl3 aims to improve urban
        livability by increasing access, coverage, quality, reliability and inter-agency coordination
        in the provision of transport, water, sanitation andrelated basic services, particularly for  .
        residents in low-income areas. Among the beneficiaries are about 600,000 low-income
        residents who live in 14 of the city's poorest zones Subprojects to be supported by the
        Bank's loan include inter alia an expansion of the Transmilenio bus system, which uses
        dedicatedbus lanes, as well as construction of bicycle paths and sidewalks, street paving,
        upgrading and repair of public parks and community facilities.              The Institutional
        Strengthening component i s aimed at improving the performance of the institutions in
        delivering urban services through activities that will help to ensure the social,
        environmental and financial sustainability of the works undertaken by the project.

    0   Integrated Mass Transit System Project. The US$250 million                      supports the
        expansion and/or construction of bus rapid transit systems in mediumand large cities to
        improve mobility along the most strategic mass transit corridors, such as Bogota, Pereira,
        Cartagena, Barranquilla, Soacha, Bucaramanga and Medellin, improving the standard of
        living of millions of Colombians, by decreasing time and costs of public transport. The
        Integrated Mass Transit System Project will (i)   develop high quality and sustainable bus
        rapid transit systems; (ii)improve accessibility for the poor though more feeder services
        to and from poor areas, as well as through fare integration inorder to decrease the cost for

  0   CartagenaWater Supply and Sewerage Environmental Management Project. The US$85
      million loan'' improves the water and sewerage services of Cartagena by expanding
      coverage, particularly in the city's poorest neighborhoods; facilitates the environmental
      cleanup of water bodies surrounding the city by providing adequatecollection, treatment,

      water and sewerage services in Cartagena by leveraging bank support to shore up the
      private sector participation model pioneered by ACUACAR, the city's water and
      sanitation utility.

  0   Water Sector Reform Assistance Project. The US$40 million 1oanI6supports the reform
      of municipal water utilities in underserved rural and urban areas in the country's
      Caribbean coastal region by involving the private sector in their management and
      expanding water and sewerage services to more people, especially the poor. In addition
      to supporting innovative private-sector participation schemes, the project provides
      technical assistance to guide municipal leaders as they incorporate the private sector in
      the management and operations of their water utilities. To obtain sub-project support,
      utilities must provide water services in poor neighborhoods of larger cities. Small town
      water utilities are eligible for project support only if a large proportion of their residents
      are low-income earners.

  0   Water and Sanitation Sector Support Project. The US$70 million loan" aims to improve
      the supply of efficient and sustainable municipal water, sewerage, and wastewater
      treatment in Colombia by providing capital investment subsidies in connection with
      coverage expansion and service quality improvement in poor areas.              The recently-
      approved project will (i)      support service-improvement related investment through
      targeted capital grants in small- and medium-size cities, and in some high poverty peri-
      urban areas of large cities served by public utilities; (ii) scale-up the involvement of the
      private sector in medium-size cities through the introduction of performance-based
      management arrangements with specialized operators; (iii)deliver appropriate water
      supply and sanitation investments in Colombia's underserved rural areas by financing the
      rehabilitation and construction of basic water and sanitation systems, including
      investments in water sourcing through wells and septic and latrine disposal; and (iv)
      improve financial viability and accountability of the participating municipalities while
      strengtheningtheir implementation capacity.

        aims to reduce the fiscal vulnerability of the state to adverse natural events by
        strengthening national capacity to manage disaster risk and by reducing vulnerability in
        key municipalities that combine high exposure to disaster risk and high contributions to
        national income and productivity. The first phase of the program, supported by a loan of
        US$260 million, will (i) strengthen technical capacity for risk monitoring at the national
        level through the upgrading of outmoded hydrologic, seismic, and volcanic detection and
        forecasting systems, and integrate them in a national system for managing and sharing
        information on disaster risk management; (ii)  support risk reduction at sectoral and sub-
        national levels by making financing available for investment inrisk reduction at the local
        government level and for sector agencies focusing on lifeline infrastructure; (iii)
        strengthen the financial and institutional capacity of the National Disaster Prevention and
        Response System to coordinate, monitor, and evaluate a comprehensive disaster risk
        reduction program; (iv) increase disaster risk awareness and preparedness, including
        preparation of emergency response plans; and (v) establish a comprehensive strategy of
        riskretention andriskfinancing at the levelof the State.

The proposed SusDevDPL operation complements these operations inits overarching support for
the government's National Development Plan, which emphasizes increased efficiency and
transparency of the state, together with enhanced societal participation in decision-making.
Together, these operations aim to improve access of essential public services (e.g., water,
sanitation and community roads) to the poorest segments of the population, establish cost
recovery and tariff structures that are socially and environmentally responsive, increase private
participation and bring in critically needed investments in these sectors, and help enhance the
efficiency of infrastructure provision and reducelogistics costs inthe economy.

78.     The Bank's work on environmental issues in Colombia likewise builds upon
opportunities provided under the Kyoto Protocol to the United Nations Framework Convention
on Climate Change through sources such as the Prototype Carbon Fund (PCF) and the Global
Environment Facility (GEF). The Jepirachi Carbon Offset Project'' and the Rio Amoya
Environmental Services Project," both financed by the PCF, offer the possibilities of reducing
carbon emissions while fostering important technology transfers to the renewable energy (e.g.,
wind, small hydro) sector. The High Andes Project, financed by a grant from the Global
Environment Facility, strengthens biodiversity conservation efforts in priority areas of the
megadiversecountry.

79.      Finally, the proposed SusDevDPL, together with the complementary Sustainable
Development Investment project, also incorporates lessons learned from the Colombia Urban
Environmental Management project, which was approved by the Board in FY96 and closed in
FY03. Important lessons learned from that project, which have been incorporated into the
development of the SusDevDPL, include:

         0   Projects should buildin a mechanism for regular inter-institutional coordination and a
             periodic redefinition of roles and competencies of environmental agencies.

LESSONSLEARNED
80.    The previous CAS was discussedby the Boardof ExecutiveDirectors inNovember 1997,
and the CAS Progress Report was discussed in November 1999. Anchored with a stable
macroeconomic environment and fiscal sustainability, CAS objectives were to be achieved by
interventions in the following key areas: (i)promoting peace and development; (ii)     supporting
rural development; (iii)developing human capital; (iv) attaining public sector responsiveness; (v)
improving infrastructure services; and (vi) ensuring sustainable development. Lessons learned
from the 1997 CAS include (i) importance of incorporating civil society in the formulation
                                 the
and implementation of development initiatives, to attain a high level of ownership and
commitment to successful project implementation; (ii)in the infrastructure sector, project
performance improved due to the implementation of a strategy to shift from direct financing of
ventures to activities that aimed at strengthening the regulatory and institutional framework to
enable private sector participation; and (iii)
                                            the Bank's investment in analytical work, especially
collaborative ESW products, provides decisive guidance in the design of operations and helps
foster a process of debate on key development issues and promotes serious discussion of
alternativesfacing the country.

81.    The current CAS was discussedby the Board of Executive Directors on January 16, 2003
and builds upon lessons learned from the 1997 CAS.            Similarly, design of the proposed
SusDevDPL benefits from these lessons, building upon a significant investment in analytical
work via the Country Environmental Analysis, strengthening the regulatory and institutional
framework with respect to the SINA, and including public consultations in terms of specific
policy reforms and inthe analytical findings of the CEA.

82.    The proposed SusDevDPL builds on the lessons learned from other programmatic
adjustment operations. The "Good Practice Notes for Development Policy Lending" (Internal
Report, October 2004), which reviews the experience with recent adjustment lending, identifies
issues of design and implementation, and suggests a number of good practices for such
operations. Inbrief, the actions that the SusDevDPL will support are part of a larger government
reform program supportedat the Presidentiallevel:

      i. Aconciseandmonitorablesetoftriggershasbeenclearlyidentified.

      ii. Ex-anteassessments of Colombia's public expenditure andfinancial management
           systems have been carried out, while a similar assessment for procurement i s
           underway.




                                                  40

    iii. The programfocusesonissuesoftransparencyoftheenvironmental management
           system.

    iv.    Risks and mitigationmeasures havebeenclearly identified.

     v.    The proposedprogrambuildson extensive analytical work. (See Table 6.)

83.     The proposed SusDevDPL is the first Development Policy Loanrelated to mainstreaming
the environment in key sectors in Colombia, but the third operation of its type in the Latin
American and Caribbean Region. It builds upon lessons learned from this limited set of
environmental reform operations, particularly from the Mexico Programmatic Environmental
Structural Adjustment Loan I(EnvSAL I)     that was approved by the Board inFY02. The second
operation, Brazil First Programmatic Reform Loan for Environmental Sustainability, was
approvedby the BoardinFY05. The Mexico EnvSALproducedthe following lessons:

      1.   Importance of a strong analytical base, which includes technical, institutional, and
           political economy analyses. In particular, identification of priorities based on both
           analytical work and perceptions; and good understanding of the institutional
           framework, includingbothinformalandformal rules.

           In the proposed SusDevDPL for Colombia, this has involved assessments of the
           institutional needs of the Ministry of Environment, Housing and Regional
           Development, the national environmental system and the Regional Autonomous
           Corporations, the latter which administer approximately 70 percent of operational
           resources within the SINA and 85percent of investment resources within the SINA.
     ..
     11.   Ensure that the weakest have a voice and are not disproportionately affected, and
           ensure that not only formal rules and institutions are reformed, but also that
           behavioral changes are brought about inthe longterm.

           The SusDevDPL promotes mechanisms for improved participation, accountability
           and transparency to influence long-term behavioral changes.           Consultations in
           preparation of the SusDevDPL and Country Environmental Analysis have been held
           with a number of civil society organizations, academics, and other concerned
           stakeholders in addition to government institutions and private sector groups.
     ...
     111.  A supporting Technical Assistance Loan is recommended since typically inter-
           sectoral coordination i s a slow process and needs continuous budgetary and technical
           support.

           The SusDevDPL focuses only on priority sectors, so that inter-institutional
           coordination efforts are not scattered but focused on the sectors that have highest
           impact in terms of environmental degradation.         A complementary Sustainable
           Development Investment project will be presented to the Board to directly support
           and strengthen the government's capacity to manage the program for results, by
           supporting SINA institutions in improving their management systems as well as
           providing technical assistance to strengthen coordination among key stakeholders,
           particularly those managing environmental health and urban development issues.



                                                  41

     iv.   Focus on a few central actions, impacts andresults inthe design of the Program.

            The SusDevDPL Program Matrix takes an integrated rather than sector-specific
           approach. Within the two principal areas of work - (i) improving the effectiveness
           and eficiency of the National Environmental System (SINA); and (ii) integrating
           principles of sustainable development into key sectors - the proposed program
           emphasizes recognition of prior actions in the first operation, implementation of
           policy reforms in the second operation, and achievement of impacts, embodied by
            indicators that reflect cross-sectoral interventions, in the third operation.

84.     Finally, program design benefits from lessons learned and outputs from the studies
contracted by the IDB, including studies linked with its recently-completed Colombia
Environmental Project together with those from the on-going SINA IIproject, both of which aim
to buildcapacity inSINA institutions.

ANALYTICAL UNDERPINNINGS
85.     The programrestson a solidfoundation, stemmingfrom abody of analytical work carried
out by the government of Colombia, the Bank, other development partners, and internationally
renowned academics. Inaddition to the policy notes preparedby the World Bank for the current
administration - Colombia: The Economic Foundation of Peace (2002) - and other economic
and sector work, the most recent work includes studies financed by the Bank as inputs to the
Country Environmental Analysis (CEA) as well as studies financed under the Japanese PHRD
grant to preparethe first operation (Table 6).

86.     The main objective of the Country Environmental Analysis (CEA) was to provide the
government with an analytical basis for decision-making on how best to improve institutional
structures for environmental management, as the government moves forward with reforms to the
SINA through changes in Law 99. The actual process of carrying out the analysis was likewise
important, as it aimed to build consensus among stakeholders on these reforms. B y achieving
this objective, the CEA provided a basis for the structure of the SusDevDPL and Sustainable
Development Investment project

87.     Inorder to achieve its objective, the CEA was carried out in a two-step manner: (i)
assisting the government to identify major environmental concerns, in the context of the
government's broader priorities, namely peace, sustainable economic growth and employment
generation, social equity and transparency and efficiency of the State, as identified in the
National Development Plan 2003 and to identify the sectors that they stem from; and (ii)    apply
the Bank's World Development Report on Sustainable Development 2003 framework to assess
more broadly across these priority areas how problems are sensed; the mechanisms to balance
interests across the different range of stakeholders; and the implementation of solutions that are
durable over time.      Cornerstones of the CEA include: (i)an assessment of the cost of
environmental degradation in Colombia; (ii)       a broad assessment of institutional capacity for
environmental management in Colombia; and (iii)           an assessment of the effectiveness and
efficiency of the existing policy, legislative and regulatory framework to address environmental
concerns. Furthermore a broad survey of societal perceptions was carried out to assist the




                                                     42

government in identifying the countries priority environmental concerns, as well as the sectors
from which they stem.

            b. The "Assessment of Colombia's National Environmental System (SINA)"
                  reviews the effectiveness of the SINA organizations, namely MAVDT, the CARS,
                  and municipal agencies and their institutional capacity to implement and enforce
                  policies and regulations inpriority areas."              The study examines the (i)           distribution
                  of responsibilities among national, regional, and local environmental authorities,
                  (ii) existence of checks and balances within environmental agencies at various


 Blackman, Allen, SandraHoffman, RichardMorgenstern,andElizabethTopping. 2004. "Assessment of Colombia's National
Environmental System (SINA)." Washington, DC: Resourcesfor the Future. Paper preparedfor the Country Environmental
Analysis.


                                                                  44

                  levels, (iii)   cross-sectoral and inter-institutional coordination mechanisms, (iv)
                  monitoring capacity, and (v) ways in which stakeholder interests are balanced in
                  decision-making processes. The "effectiveness" of an institution is defined as the
                  extent to which it performs the functions assigned to it by Colombia's 1991
                  Constitution, Law 99 of 1993 and related Decrees, and thereby contributes to
                  improved environmental quality.
                           Principal problems within the SINA identified in the study include inter
                  alia: (i)  Inadequate regulations. For example, command-and-control emissions
                  standards have sometimes been adoptedfrom more developed countries with little
                  or no modification; (ii)        Limited technical capacity in MAVDT and some CARs;
                  (iii) Regulatory capture. Evidence suggests that interest groups exert undue
                  influence on the activities of environmental authorities at both the national and
                  regional levels, so that instead of acting to further social welfare, they act to
                  further the interests of select groups; (iv) Inadequate enforcement. For example,
                  of the effluent fees that CARs charge to polluters, only one-third are actually
                  collected; (v) Inadequate data on environmental quality and institutional
                  performance; (vi) Lack of priority setting across environmental sub-sectors and
                  programs; and (vii) Inadequatemechanismsfor public participation.

              c. The "Institutional Analysis of Colombia's Autonomous Regional Corporations
                  (CARs)" reviews the evolution of the CARs, particularly over the past decade
                  since passage of Law 99, as well as identifies good practices and lessons learned
                  which may be applied more widely on improving the environmental management
                  functions of the CARS." Among others, the study (i)                  describes the institutional
                  and social context of the CARs, and assesses aspects of the social setting and
                  institutional factors likely to impact upon the quality of environmental
                  management; (ii)       examines the effectiveness in the design and implementation of
                  policies and procedures used for environmental management by the CARs; (iii)
                  evaluates the effectiveness of the CARs in terms of their major environmental
                  functions; and (iv) identifies major institutional, administrative, and political
                  challenges facing the regional environmental regulatory system in carrying out
                  their environmental mandate. Principal findings and recommendations may be
                  found inparagraphs43 - 53 above.

              d. The "Colombian Environmental Priorities" study included a survey of more than
                  2,600 Colombians throughout the country, including numerous sectors,
                  governmental authorities, civil society representativesand various ethnic
                  Air pollution is identified as a very serious problem by 79 percent of persons
                  surveyed, followed by global environmental problems (73%), deforestation
                  (65%), noise pollution (65%) and vulnerability to natural disasters (65%).
                  However, the study illustrates significant differences in priorities between groups


22 Blackman,Allen, RichardMorgenstern, and ElizabethTopping. 2004. "Institutional Analysis of Colombia's Autonomous
RegionalCorporations (CARS)." Washington, DC: Resourcesfor the Future. Paper preparedfor the Country Environmental
Analysis.

23 Centro Nacionalde Consultorfa. 2004. "Prioridades Ambientales Colombianos." Bogoti, Colombia: CentroNacionalde
Consultoria. Surveypreparedfor the Country EnvironmentalAnalysis.


                                                              45

                  surveyed; for instance, whereas low-income groups identify air pollution, noise
                  pollution and vulnerability to natural disasters as major problems, upper income
                  groups perceive the loss of global environmental resources (e.g., biodiversity,
                  global warming) and inappropriate landuse inurban areas as principalproblems.
                           Survey results reflect a potential bias in environmental expenditures in
                  Colombia. Colombia is highly urbanized, with 72 percent of the population in
                  urban areas. Close to 50 percent of the population lives in cities with more than
                  100,000 inhabitants, and close to 30 percent live in cities with more than one
                  million inhabitants. However, expenditures on environmental management focus
                  disproportionately on natural resources, forestry and biodiversity, and tend to pay
                  much less attention to urban environmental issues affecting the poor, such as air
                  quality, water quality, and solid and hazardous waste management.24 The
                  "Assessment         of   Colombia's       National Environmental            System       (SINA)"
                  corroborates this "green bias" theory.                 The report evaluated Ministry of
                  Environment investment (versus operational) spending and found that "green"
                  issues (i.e., related to natural resources management) accounted for seventy-five
                  percent of the Ministry's investment budget, while "brown" issues (e.g. air
                  quality, water quality) accountedfor only twenty-five percent of investments.

88.      Additional studies and reports that have contributed to the design of this program range
from the recent Public Environmental Expenditure Review conducted by the Economic
Commission of Latin America (ECLAC) to studies commissioned by the government to assess
the effectiveness of various SINA institutions, as well as broader institutional assessments at the
request of the government by international authorities, including the recently published
Institutional Reforms in Colombia.




24The adjective "green" is used inenvironmental policy to refer to natural resource issues such as forest and
biodiversity conservation while the adjective "brown" is used to refer to urban environmental degradation issues
such air pollution, water pollution, and hazardous and solid waste.

   V.      THE PROPOSEDCO PROGRAMMATICDEVELOPMENTPOLICYLOAN
                                FORSUSTAINABLEDEVELOPMENT

OBJECTIVESOFTHE PROGRAM
89.     The overarching objective of the proposed Sustainable Development DPL Program
(comprising of three operations, SusDevDPL I,     SusDevDPL 11and SusDevDPL III)to support
                                                                                       i s
the government of Colombia's efforts to move towards achievement of the Millennium
Development Goals (MDGs), particularly Goal 7 which seeks to ensure environmental
sustainability, Target 9 which aims to integrate the principles of sustainable development into
country policies and programs, Target 10 which aims to reduce the proportion of people without
sustainable access to safe drinking water and basic sanitation, and Target 11 which aims to
achieve a significant improvement in the lives of the urban poor. Furthermore, by addressing
urban environmental issues affecting the most vulnerable populations (e.g., air pollution,
untreated drinking water), the program supports achievement of Goal 4 which seeks to reduce
child mortality through addressing principal threats to children under five (e.g., morbidity and
mortality related to respiratory illnesses and diarrheal diseases). Specifically, the program to be
supported has the following objectives: (i)     improving the effectiveness and efficiency of the
(Sistema Nacional Ambiental, SINA) in Colombia; and (ii)        integrating principles of sustainable
development into key sectors, with a particular emphasis on protecting the most vulnerable
groups.

90.     These objectives would be met through a series of actions, which include developing a
results-based framework for planning and monitoring progress of the SINA towards goals
directly linked with sustainable development and achievement of the MDGs; improvements in
inter-institutional coordination and increased public participation in environmental decision-
making; preparation of critical policies and laws that embody sustainable development; and
preparation of critical policies and laws related to air quality, water quality, solid waste
management, andenvironmental licensing.

CONTENTOFTHE PROGRAM
91.     The operation will be based on a subset of the government's overall medium-term,
outcome-based, sequenced 2002-2006 National Development Program and presented in the
government's Letter of Development Policy. Key milestones have been selectedas commitments
for respective operations. The program aims to facilitate the development and implementation of
policies, plans, andinstruments ina selective and flexible manner.

92.     Jointly-defined criteria for selection of sectors to be supported under the loan, include the
linkage with (i) cost of environmental degradation, measuredinterms of economic and health
                 the
impacts; (ii) provision of basic needs (e.g., clean air, water, waste management) for the poor; (iii)
opportunities based on institutional reorganization, namely the merging of environment with
housing, water and sanitation, and territorial development under a single Ministry; and (iv)
financial constraints and the need to use limited financial resources to achieve as great an impact
as possible.

93.     The commitments under the program are listed in the Program Matrix (See Annex 2).
The program is designed in such a way that the first operation focuses on prior actions to (i)
develop a results-based framework for planning and monitoring progress of the SlNA towards


                                                    48

goals directly linkedwith sustainable development and achievement of the MDGs; (ii)                      improve
inter-institutional coordination mechanisms and increase public participation indecision-making
to promote greater transparency and accountability in governmental decision-making processes;
and (iii) preparation of critical laws and decrees related to water resourcesmanagement,
            initiate
air quality, solid waste management, and environmental licensing. The secondoperation focuses
on the development and passage of sustainable development policies, with the third operation
focusing in particular on the results of implementation of these policies and instruments,
embodied in achieving indicators closely linkedto the MDGs.

CONTENT OF THELOAN
94.       The disbursement of the loan would support initial actions taken by the government inits
program outlined in the Letter of Development Policy. The loan of US$150 million would be
disbursed in a single tranche upon loan effectiveness. The conditions against which the funds
would be transferred are detailed inBox 5 below.

95,       The actions detailed under this single-tranche operation highlight critical aspects in the
government's program to move towards achievement of MDGs, specifically Goals 7, 4 and 1,
namely through:

         i. developing a results-based framework for planning and monitoring progress of the
             SINA towards goals directly linked with sustainable development and achievement of
             the MDGs. This would be carried out by changing the emphasis of the three-year plans,
            currently prepared by the Regional Autonomous Corporations (CARs), from
             administrative and process-based commitments to commitments directly linked with
             impact, namely MDG-linked indicators. This will enable better alignment of national
             and regional priorities, assist the CARs to be more strategic as they fulfill their mandate
             as promoters of regional sustainable development and key actors in achieving the
            MDGs, promote improvedinter-institutional coordination between sectors at a regional
             level and vertically between national andregional authorities, and greater accountability
             and transparency with respect to monitoring the effectiveness and efficiency of
             environmental managementperformance at the regional level;

        ii. improvements in inter-institutional coordination and increased public participation in
             environmental decision-making. This would be accomplished through changes in the
             existing Technical Advisory Council,25 to reduce regulatory capture, through ensuring
            broad-based and qualified professional participation that ensures that this Council
            provides a forum for cross-sectoral coordination and for ensuring that the most
             vulnerable groups (affected by environmental degradation) have a voice in
            environmental decision-making.                In addition,        modifications    to the current
            environmental licensing scheme are expected to improve transparency and
             accountability by clarifying the rules and opening up decision-making to public
            participation; and




25This Council is apart of the SINA and was established through Law 99 of 1993. Itis headed by the Vice-Minister
of the Environment and includes representatives from the agricultural, mining, manufacturing and oil sectors. Its
role is to advise the Minister on the design of environmental policy and regulation.


                                                             49

       iii.preparationofcriticalpoliciesandlawsrelatedtoairquality, waterquality, solidwaste
               management, and environmental licensing. This would include the preparation and
               presentation to Congress of a new water resources management law that encompasses
               all aspects of water management, including the right of access to water, water
               availability and coverage and water quality.            Second, issuance of a decree on the
               management and final disposal of solid wastes to reduce degradation of water
               resources. Third, design and approval of an action plan for the formulation of a new air
               pollution control policy, which emphasizes (i)         measurement of key pollutants that are
               associated with significant health impacts; (ii)      research to better understand the causal
               linksbetween health impacts andpollutants; and(iii)          promotion of measures to mitigate
               these health impacts inbothrural and urban settings. Finally, approval by the CONPES
               of an integrated sustainable development policy that brings together housing, regional
               development, transport and environmental issues, with the aim of reducing the
               disproportionate burden that is borne by the poor of the cost of environmental
               degradation.
