                                                                                          Bogota
                                                                         Thursday, April 14,2005


Mr. JAMES D. WOLFENSOHN
President
WORLD BANK
Washington, D.C.


Dear Mr. Wolfensohn,

The Government of Colombia remains committed to defining a wide-ranging program of World
Bank loans for the 2003-2006 period, which represent one of the most persuasive
demonstrations of support for the country. Announcements of such programs by multilateral
development agencies, in particular the World Bank and the Inter-AmericanDevelopmentBank,
have helpedto restore confidence in the stability of the Colombianeconomy, and to re-establish
access to world markets. Furthermore,this display of confidence has been essential in enabling
the Government to introduce its ambitious program of reforms, a good many of which have
already been passed by the Colombian Congress.

Fiscal stability and the implementation of structural reform, that allow all Colombians to benefit
from the opportunities inherent in a healthy economy, are among the pillars of the present
administration.Within this context of adjustment and reform, the administration is committedto
the provision of basic social services and protectionof the country's most vulnerable segments
of the population..

In this same context, the Government of Colombia continues to work towards securing and
mobilizing resources under conditions favorable to the country. These resources will support
adjustments in sectoral public policy and the implementation of institutional change. They will
also act to cushion against any immediate adverse effects of the fiscal adjustment currently
underway.

Within its framework of medium- and long-term economic and social policy, the Government is
designing a package of institutional and policy reforms to promote Sustainable Development
that will enable the Government of Colombia to move towards achievement of the Millennium
Development Goals, in particular Goal 7, which seeks to ensure environmental sustainability,
and Goals 1 and 4, which seek to reduce povertyand infant mortality, respectively.To ensure a
successful outcome, the Government of Colombia is hereby requesting financial support from
the Bank, as well as technical assistance and an on-going partnership in the design of
mechanismsto support such reforms.



                                                  80

The accompanying document describes the background, context and conceptual framework in
which this operation will be developed for Colombia. The policies incorporated therein stem
from an extensive exchange of ideas between teams from both the World Bank and the
Colombian Government, including the Ministry of Finance and Public Credit, Department of
National Planning,, and Ministry of Environment, Housing and Regional Development.This
dialogue has resulted in the development of a program that will allow the Government to move
towards achievement of the Millennium DevelopmentGoals.

Lastly, the Government expresses its firm commitment to the program and would welcome a
partnership with, and financial support from, the World Bank in this endeavor. The Government
of Colombia also expresses its appreciation to the Bank's Board of Directors for its early
consideration of this request for a Programmatic Development Policy Loan for Sustainable
Development,in the amount of US$150 millionfor 2005.

Cordiallyyours,

                         1.  ENSURING MACROECONOMICSTABILITY


1. Following a difficult period of recession between 1998 and 1999, the Colombian economy
   has taken a rapid upturn, reflected both in an increase in the domestic product growth rate
   and in private sector dynamism.

2. This process has been fostered by noteworthyachievements in the fiscal arena, resulting in
   improvedfinancial conditionsthat, in turn, have restoredinvestor confidence.

3. During 2003, the country experienced accelerated economic growth, which increasedfrom
   1.9% in 2002 to 3.84% in 2003. Such growth is more consistent with the economy's long-
   term potential.The Governmenthopes economic growth in 2004 will be around 4%.

4. This level of growth was driven in large part by the dynamism in domestic demand. Indeed,
  while domestic demand registered an increase of 4.9% during 2003, this same welcome
   trend continued from January through September 2004, registering a 4.64% increase.
   Increasing activity in domestic investment plus favorable external conditions benefited all
   sectors in the economy, including employment, with the 2004 unemployment rate falling to
   13.6%, down from 14.1% in 2003. The investment dynamic plus the reduction in
   unemployment, have in turn brought about an increase in productivecapacity.

5. This dynamic growth has not led to any excessive pressure on the stock market, nor on
   external financing. In the case of the stock market, inflation in 2004 stood at 5.50%, down
   one percentage point from the 2003 level. As regards external financing, the current
   account deficit stood at 1.5% of the Gross Domestic Product (GDP) for the period January-
   September 2004, with a deficit of 1.l% of GDP projectedfor the end of the year, down from
   the 2003 deficit (1.5% of GDP). These factors make it possible to predict that the
   reactivation process is likely to be sustained.

6. The fiscal deficit fell from 3.6% of GDP in 2002, to 2.7% of GDP in 2003; in other words,
   approximately one percentage point in one year. This fiscal deficit reduction is the result of
   an enormous effort being made by all entities within the public sector, among which the
   National Governmentdeserves special mentionfor reducing its deficit by 0.7%.

7. These positive results-the fruit of the applied fiscal policy-have played a significant role in
   achieving greater macroeconomic equilibrium and in meeting fiscal targets. They have
   enabledthe Governmentto comply with its agreement made with the InternationalMonetary
   Fund (IMF), to reduce the Consolidated Public Sector deficit to the level of 2.7% of GDP.
   The increase in GDP, the dynamic of the public sector's primary balance, and the positive
   result in interest rates as a consequence of the two aforementioned factors, led to a
   reduction in the net total debt from 53.9% in 2002 to 51.2% in 2003. The debt level
   projectedfor the end of 2004 is 46.3% of GDP, which is 4 percentage points lower than the
   level projected in the Fiscal Frameworkfor the MediumTerm.



                                                  84

8. The reduction of the Non-Financial Public Sector's debt as a percentage of GDP reversed
   the trend registered in recent years. Had the trend been maintained, it would have posed a
   serious threat to the Government'ssolvency.

9. On the external front, Colombia's position remains solid. This has made it possible for the
   Government to maintain its current account balance, and to increase foreign reserves to a
   level of USD 13,743.7 milliondollars, calculatedup to January 2005.

10.The Government met the fiscal deficit goal of 0.7% of GDP agreed with the IMF for the third
   quarter of 2004. By that third quarter, there was an accumulated surplus for the
   Consolidated Public Sector of 0.9% of GDP. This result reflects the continuation of the
   Central Government's adjustment policy, and an improvement in the decentralized public
   sector. For its part, the National Central Government registered a deficit of 2.8% of GDP,
   0.6% lower than the corresponding deficit for the third quarter of 2003. As well as reflecting
   the adjustment policy,this result also reflects greater efforts being made in tax collection.

11.The fiscal deficit goal for 2004 was set at 2.5% of GDP, but this has been reviseddownward
   to 2.3% of GDP, as a result of the Central Government's improved performance and of
   savings in the Fund for Savings and Oil Stabilization (FAEP) provided by a change in oil
   prices. Preliminary estimates indicate that the Central Public Sector deficit for 2004 will be
   lower than the revised goal of 2.3% of GDP, even perhaps falling below 2.0% of GDP.

12.A fiscal deficit of 2.5% of GDP is expected for 2005. To reach this goal, a primary surplus of
   2.6% of GDP, will be needed in the Non-Financial Public Sector. This fiscal deficit goal
   includes priority investment projects in infrastructure, the high economic yield from which
   guaranteesthat debt sustainability will not be affected.

13. For 2006, the Government hopes to have a Consolidated Public Sector deficit close to 2.0%
   of GDP.

14.The Government's program continues to be oriented towards guaranteeing fiscal
   sustainability and, in the last instance, to improving security and economic growth, and
   increasing social equity. The Government is, therefore, continuing to implement the
   measures required to achieve these objectives within an environment of macroeconomic
   stability, supported by prudent monetary policies and a reinforcement of the financial
   system. At the same time, the Government is working to strengthen its social welfare
   programs,to broaden coverage of health and education services, and to mitigate the impact
   of publicservice tariff increaseson the poorest segments of the population.

15.The Government is well aware of the urgent need to continue with its plan for structural
    reforms, in order to continue its policy of gradual fiscal adjustment and to guarantee
   productionof primary surpluses that will make Colombia's debt sustainable in the longterm.

16.The legislative agenda is therefore continuing its attack on the principal fiscal risk, which is
   pensions. Activities intended to attain this control are directed towards limiting spending
   pressures. In this context, the Government intendsto modify the pension regime, in order to
   avoid future fiscal pressure. Among the key points in the Legislative Bill, which had its first
   debate in Congresson October 13, 2004, are:

   0   Establishinga maximum pension amount of 25 times the minimum legal wage, from the
       date when the Bill becomes Law


                                                   85

       Elimination of regimesfor exceptionalor special pensions, except the regime applicable
       to the fuerzapublica
   e   Eliminationof Mesada 74for new pensioners
       Bringing forwardthe date for the end of the transition regime

17. Inthe legislativeagenda, considerationwas given to other relevantaspects, such as:

        0   ABudget Law
        0   Analysis and approval of a Bill to establish norms for standardizing the State
            portfolio
            A Projectto reduce bureaucraticprocedures




                                                 86

               II. INSTITUTIONALAND ENVIRONMENTALPOLICY FRAMEWORK



18.Over the last three (3) decades, Colombia has introduced numerous policy initiatives and
   strengthened its institutional capacity to protect and manage the country's natural
   resources. The first Regional Autonomous Corporation in Colombia (CAR), the Regional
   Autonomous Corporation of the Cauca Valley (CVC), was created in 1954 to promote
    integrated economic development in the Cauca Valley. During the 1960s and 1970s vital
   environmental regulations were passed, including the 1969 Forestry Law, the 1977 Statute
   creatingthe National System of Natural National Parks, and the1973 Flora Statute.

19.The most important new regulation was the Code of Renewable Natural Resources and
    Environmental Protection, an integrated statute that remains one of the key pillars of
   environmental and natural resources legislation in Colombia. The Code was one of the
   principal laws in the world on environmental protection to incorporate environmental taxes
   and environmental impact assessments. During the 1980s, Colombia also designed and
    implemented regulationsfor both air and water pollution control.

20. The new PoliticalConstitutionestablished in 1991, and Law 99 passed in 1993,fostered the
   consolidation of the National Environment System in Colombia (SINA). Created in 1993, the
    SlNA constitutes one of the most singular environmental institutionalframeworks, and one
   with the greatest potential in Latin America, in that it proposes democratic, decentralized
    and participatory environmentalmanagement. The SlNA has been defined as the integrated
   framework for environmental management in the country. It is presided over by the Ministry
    of Environment, Housing and Regional Development (MAVDT), the body responsible for
    environmental policy and regulation in Colombia. The SlNA comprises a group of 33
    regional environmental institutions-Regional         Autonomous Corporations (CARS)-which
    cover the country as a whole, in addition to four Research Institutes, the Urban
    Environmental Authorities, and other regional and local institutions. Since 1994, significant
    resources from the National Budget have been assigned to environmental protection, even
    though the SINAs primary source of financing originates from the CAR'S own resources.
    For the 1995 to 2002 period, out of a total approaching USD 1,700 million in SlNA
    resources, 31% came from the National Budget, with the remaining 69% provided by CAR
    revenues. With respect to CAR resources, 66.7% were spent on investments, and the
    remainderto meet recurrent costs.

21. Despite the significance of CAR revenue to environmental management,' there is a marked
    inequality in the distribution of this income. Indeed, 74% of the total is concentrated in only
    eight (8) CARs, while the remaining twenty-five (25) CARs only generate 26%. The main
    profit-generatingsources for the CARs, are: (i) a percentage of the municipal property tax
    (35% of total income); (ii) investment yield (31Yo); (iii) direct transfers from companies
    generating hydroelectric power (10%); (iv) inter-institutional agreements plus collections on

'For 2003, the CARsown profits were close to USD259 million (including capital profits).



                                                     87

   the use and enjoyment of natural resources (10%); and (v) pollutionfees (2%). Only in parts
   of the country where urban and economic development are low, do the respective CARs
    depend heavilyon funding from the country's General Budget.

22. Achievements in environmental matters over the last thirty years have centered on natural
    resource conservation and deforestation control. During this period, significant
    environmental goals have been achieved, such as the establishmentof the Natural National
    Parks (covering 8.5% of the national territory) and Nature Reserves (on more than 15% of
    the national territory). The Government has also been working to eliminate lead from
    gasoline. Recently, local environmental achievements have also focused on urban
    development. For example, at the end of the 199Os, the Municipal Government of Bogota
    introduced an integrated program to improve the quality of city life. The program included:
    (i) a change in the construction scheme for roads to accommodate an efficient mass
    transport system, sidewalks and bicycle lanes; (ii) an increase in education levels by more
    than 30%; and (iii) the construction or remodellingof 1,243 parks which are currently visited
    by more than 1.5 million people each year

23. The new challenges arising from the Sustainable Development Summit in Johannesburg
    and the Millennium Development Goals, as well as the environmental issues described
    below in Chapter Ill, motivated Colombia to consider a long-term perspective,involving the
    integration of the environment into sectoral development agendas. Current world trends
    demand from the country, modern environmental policies, the establishment of an
    appropriate distributionof responsibilities,and the utilizationof new instruments.

24. To meet these needs, the National Government finds itself in a process of formulating and
    executing public policiesfor adjustment and institutionalchange. Examplesof this course of
    action include: a better alignment of inter-sectoral actions; the adoption of integrated
    strategies; the development of appropriate instruments to ensure greater efficiency in
    environmental management; the promotion of transparency; support for regional
    development; and a framework of standards and administrative procedures in line with
    today's realities.

25. Within this framework, Colombia has decided to redirect its environmental policy priorities,
    orientating them towards guaranteeing sustainable growth and the reductionof poverty. The
    country's environmental policy seeks, therefore, to accelerate the integration of
    environmental issues into the agendas of priority sectors (for example, health, housing and
    regional development, water, urban development, energy, and transport). The aim is to
    address the environmental problems that affect the most vulnerable segments of the
    population. Since August 2002, the Government has been working to define strategies for
    promoting sustainable development in line with its National Environmental Policy, and to
    guarantee their effective incorporation into local and regional planning and policy initiatives
    relatedto strategic sectors.

26. To demonstrate the National Government's commitment to achieving the Millennium
    Development Goals, the following actions have been taken: (i) the issuance of National
    Development Plan (PND) 2002-2006, "Towards a Community State," which seeks to
    strengthen environmental aspects within Colombia's national priorities; (ii) the creation of
    the Ministry of the Environment, Housing and Regional Development (MAVDT), which will
    provide significant opportunities for integrating the environment into key sectors, such as
    infrastructure, water and housing; (iii) the establishment of a system of indicators to
    measure the results and impact made by the organizations executing the National
    Environment Policy, i.e., the Regional Autonomous Corporations (CARs); as well as a



                                                   88

    Presidential Decree setting out guidelines for open public election of CAR Directors; (iv) an
    agenda of regulated development and policies to address priority themes of environmental
    degradation, caused by air and water pollution plus the inadequate disposal of solid wastes,
    with this agenda to be finalized in the next two years.

27. The National Congress has passed the National Development Plan (PND) 2002-2006,
    "Towards a Community State," into Law. This Plan has allowed President Uribe's
    Administration to introduce structural reforms for more effective inclusion of priority
    environmental issues into sectoral policies, in accordance with the Government's
    commitment to meet the Millennium DevelopmentGoals.

28. From the institutional point of view, the Colombian Government has integrated the greater
    part of the functions of the Ministry of Economic Development (responsible for policies on
    housing, land use, drinking water, and basic and environmentalsanitation) with those of the
    Ministry of the Environment, to create the new Ministry of Environment, Housing and
    Regional Development (MAVDT). As regards water, in the past, institutions responsible for
    integrated water management were distributed among a wide group of national, regional
    and local bodies. Recent state reform introducedby the present Government has created a
    central institutional platform, to ensure greater efficiency and coordination in the
    development of water policies and regulations. Under this reform, MAVDT's Vice Ministry of
    Environment is responsible for environmental functions concerning integrated water
    management, including policies related to potable water and basic and environmental
    sanitation.

29. The National Development Plan (PND) intentionally has no specific chapter on the
    environment. Instead, it introduces and strengthens environmental aspects within the
    country's national priorities, aiming thereby to make progress in democratic security,
    economic growth, employment, social equity and public participation. The PND effectively
    integratesthe environment into policies and programs,aimed at reversingthe destructionof
    natural resources and improving access to environmental services. Regarding the
    environment, and to boost sustainable economic growth with the generationof em lo ment,
    the PND has been based on the Strategy of Environmental Sustainability!                          :his  is
    evidenced in five programs the State has begun implementing: (i) conservation and
    sustainable use of environmental goods and services, (ii) integratedwater management, (iii)
    generationof income and "green" employment, (iv) sustainability of national production, and
    (v) efficient environmentalplanning and administration..

30. The following complementarystrategies related to additional PND environmental objectives
    have also been adopted: (i) In an effort to promote Social Equity, the PND aims to provide
    wider and improved protection and social welfare through priority actions in the fields of
    public health, quality of urban life, and the preventionand mitigationof natural disaster risk;
    and (ii) In an effort to increasethe transparency and efficiencyof the State, the PND aims to
    decentralize and improve organization of regional development, by designing appropriate
    planningand managementinstruments, and by promotingbetter communication betweenall
    government departments involved.

31. The Ministry of Environment, Housing and Regional Development (MAVDT) formulated its
    Sectoral Plan for 2002 - 2006, to define priorities that foster sustainable development. The


 In different arenas, such as the Global Economic Forum (Davos, 2001), Colombia is listed number 36 among 122
nations in regard to environmental sustainability; and number 13, according to NGOs such as Greenpeace and
Friendsof the Earth (RodriguezM.,2002, Colombia's Environmental Future).


                                                         89

   Plan's main objectives are to increase the provision of basic requirements such as potable
   water, basic sanitation, access to adequate housing, and biodiversity protection. MAVDT is
   seeking thereby to integrate environmentalconsiderations into the development agendas of
   key sectors, and to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of environmental management
   in Colombia.

32.This Sectoral Plan is dependent on sizeable resourcesfrom international banks, sought
   jointly by MAVDT in co-ordinationwith the Ministryof Financeand Public Credit and the
    National Planning Department.As such, the following World Bank operations have been
   programmed: (i) ProgrammaticDevelopmentPolicy Loanfor Sustainable Development
   (SusDevDPL),USD 300 million (the first operation being USD 150million); (ii) Investment
    Loanfor Sustainable Development (IDS), USD7 million; (iii) Credit to finance in part the
    Programto Assist in Reform of the PotableWater Sector, USD 40 million; (iv) a Support
    Programfor the Water and Sanitation Sector, USD 70 million; (v) a Creditto finance a
   program to Reducethe State's FiscalVulnerability in the event of Natural Disaster, USD
   260 million; and (vi) a Credit for an IntegratedMass Transport System, USD250 million.
   Similarly, credits have also been sought from the IDBto finance a National Environmental
   System Support Program (SINA ll),USD 20 million; and a Social Housing Policy, USD 200
    million.

33. Of the programs mentioned above, the SusDevDPL stands out due to its future structural
    implications. This credit forms part of the National Government's strategy to seek freely
   available resources, under conditions favorable to Colombia. As a result of the SusDevDPL,
   the Government has implemented reforms and adjustments in the environmental field, by
    passing Laws, decrees and/or policies: (i) to improve the efficiencyand effectivenessof the
    SINA; and (ii) to promote integration of sustainable development principles in strategic
   sectors.




                                                   90

                                      111. ENVIRONMENTALCHALLENGES



34. Despite institutional progress in the creation and consolidation of the SINA, Colombia
      continues to face enormous challenges in the degradation of its soil, air and water
      resources, which are compromising the country's potential for sustainable economic
      development.

35. Colombia is a highly urbanized country, with about 32 million inhabitants living in urban
      areas, which is 72% of the national population. Recent projections suggest that, in the next
      ten years, 40 million inhabitants will be living in urban areas, and 10 million in rural areas.
      Among the main problems facing Colombian cities are those due to air pollution, water-
      borne diseases, noise, the predominanceof precarious settlements in the peripherie~,~the
      scarcity of inhabitable land, irrational expansion,and a deteriorationand depopulationof the
      urban centers. In terms of the community's perceptions regardingenvironmental priorities, a
      recent survey by the Centro Nacional de Con~ultoria,~             revealedthat 74% of a sample of the
      low-incomepopulationqualifiedthe problem of air pollutionas "very serious" and 64% of the
      same group consideredthe issue of naturaldisasters also as "very serious."

36. Even though ample evidence exists internationally of the impact environmental degradation
      has on the quality of life and wellbeing of the population, Colombia does not have
      consolidated information that would allow a systematic evaluation of associated economic
      costs. This is because environmental and health information systems do not have
      sufficiently robust statistical databases to carry out reliable analyses of the effects of
      environmental variables (such as air quality) on public health. Nor are there any on-going
      epidemiologicalstudies of the scope requiredfor analysis of the problem.

37. To address the issue, and to create a better analytical base on which to evaluate priority
      environmental problems, their social and economic impact, and the relationship between
      these problems and key sectors of the economy, MAVDT is carrying out a series of
      specialized studies5These studies have demonstrated that substantial links exist between
      environmental problems and a decline in the wellbeing and quality of life of the population
      (Larsen, 2004): that environmental degradation significantly affects the health of children
      (particularly those under five [5]) and the poorest segments of the population; and
      generates an impact on the productivity of the workforce because of the inequitable way in
      which these impacts affect the most vulnerable groups. Inadequate drinking water and
      sanitation provision, air pollution, natural disasters, and indoor air pollution are the main
      causes of mortality and morbidity, predominantly affecting women and children from poor
      families.


 I t is estimated that at least 1.3 million homes (16% of all urban homes), are in precarious condition. Inother words,
they are dwellings without basic services, constructed of inadequate materials, overcrowded or built in high-risk
areas.
 Colombian Environmental Priorities, a study contracted by the World Bank, as part of the Country Environmental
Analysis, CEA (2004).
 These studies, undertaken with the support of the World Bank, as part o f the CEA, comprise an institutional
assessmentof the SINA and CAR, an evaluation of the effectiveness of national environment policy, and an
estimation o f the costs of environmental degradation and mitigation in Colombia.


                                                               91

38. As part of the CEA studies (Country EnvironmentalAnalysis), consideration must be given
     to trends observe`d in recent MAVDT studies, regarding the costs of environmental
     degradation in Colombia.` The studies indicate the most significant costs facing the country
     are related to air and water pollution, as well as the overall inadequate conditions of water,
     sanitation and hygiene more generally.

39. Evaluations in recent studies carried out as part of the CEA substantiate the need for more
     profound analysis of the effects of environmental degradation in Colombia, in order to
     establish the most evident relationships of cause-and-effect. This, in turn, will enable
     mechanisms and/or measuresto be decided for a more cost-effective management. These
     CEA studies also provideelementsfor prioritizinginterventionsin environmental policy.

40. The impactsof environmentaldegradation in Colombia thus described are directly relatedto
     policy in sectors strategic to the economic development of the country. This is most critically
     evidenced in urban areas. One example of this relationship is the incidence of the use of
     fossil fuels, which is one of the major causal factors where air quality levels are exceeded,
     bearing in mind priority parameters such as particulate matter (PM 2.5).7 Consumption of
     these fuels depends to a large degree on policy guidelines and price signals from the
     energy sector, as well as on transport policy.

41, Air pollution is a serious problem widely prevalent in Colombian cities and industrial
     corridors. The high level of urbanization in the country effectively means a large percentage
     of the population is exposed to air pollution, and their health is thus more seriously
     affected.' Calculations estimate that 56% of the population live in the country's seven
     principal industrial centers, and that these centers produced 75% of the total national
     emissions from manufacturinggin 1999. Of the 18 known monitoring networks,the Institute
     of Hydrology, Meteorology and Environmental Studies (IDEAM) only has access to the
     measurements taken by six networks," and then only for some pollutants. This restricts to
     the quality of information and the possibility of nationwide collection. On the basis of
     available information, however, articulate matter has been identified as one of the main
     pollutants exceedingset norms.        IP

42. Even though air pollution levels are moderate in most cities, the concentration of the
        population in urban areas causes an aggregate effect in terms of health, especially in

permitted daily norm.

      association with particulate matter. The result is health impacts such as acute respiratory
      infections (especially in children who are the most vulnerable segment of the population),
      and morbidity related to illnesses such as pneumonia. Preliminary analyses reveal a
      greater part of costs due to urban air pollution being associated with mortality, which is
      estimated to occur at rates close to 6,000 deaths a year from this cause alone (Larsen,
      2004). There is also a marked underestimation of the impacts on mortality due to air
      pollution in indoor environments (1,100 deaths per yearI2), related to the use of fuelwood
      for cooking (about 65% of the rural populationstill cooks with solid fuels).

43. In terms of mortality-related costs, Larsen's study indicates that the greatest impacts are
      associated with water-borne illnesses (nearly USD 480 million annually). Although
      Colombia has mana9ed to reduce infant mortality substantially (including mortality due to
      diarrhoeal illnesses1 ), the figures quoted above show that costs are still high in terms of
      infant mortality due to air pollution and poor hygiene. The mortality rate in children under
      five (5), due to acute diarrhoeal illness is 5.6 per 100,000, and 8.4 per 100,000 due to
      acute respiratory infection. About 9% of the population has an inadequate water supply,
      and the absence of proper drainage in 20% of the urban centers is a serious
      environmentalproblemfor the country.

44. Colombia also faces numerous natural disasters, such as flooding, drought and
      earthquakes. In the last 25 years, the country has suffered six large earthquakes, three
      volcanic eruptions, and three avalanches, with a corresponding loss of human life and
      physical infrastructure for the country. According to a recent study, more than four (4)
       million Colombians were affected by natural disasters between 1993 and 2000, at an
      annual cost of approximately USD 453 million (Echeverri Garzon, 2002). The poorest and
       most vulnerable have paid the highest price for these disasters, in terms of material
      damage, deaths and loss of assets.

45. In 2002, a total of 190,000 road traffic accidents were registered in Colombia, with about
       43,000 cases of personal injury and more than 6,000 deaths. The total number of
       accidents rose from 1980 to 2001, almost doubling between 1990 and 2001. There was,
       however, a 20% reduction in the number of accidents between 2001 and 2002. Among
      the deaths due to traffic accidents, almost 40% are pedestrians and 25% are cyclists or
       motorcyclists.

lower than the averagefor lower middle-income countries.


                                                         93

                            IV. STRUCTURALADJUSTMENT STRATEGY



46. The central objective of the SusDevDPL is to support the Government of Colombia in its
    efforts to meet the MillenniumDevelopment Goals, especially those related to Objective No.
    7, seeking to ensure environmentalsustainability, and to ObjectivesNo. 1 and 4, seeking to
    reduce poverty and infant mortality, respectively. This Program would enable the
    Government to carry out a series of reforms through the adoption of Laws, decrees or
    policies: (i) to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of the SINA, and (ii) to promote the
    integrationof principlesof sustainabledevelopment into strategicsectors.

47. In order to support the implementation of SusDevDPL policy measures, the Colombian
    Government is preparing an operation, a Sustainable Development Investment Loan (IDS).
    The IDS will be used to support the implementationof a group of transversal actions, that
    will take advantage of the synergies of other sectors such as energy, health, transport, and
   from progress in urban and regionaldevelopment. Complementaryto the above, actions are
    also required to prevent and mitigate the impact of environmental degradation on the
    population's wellbeing and quality of life. There follows below a brief diagnosis of some of
    these priority areas.

48. Policy strategies related to sustainable development will be contained in a document to be
    submitted to the National Council of Economic and Social Policy (CONPES) during the first
    quarter of 2005. The policy document will take the following considerationsinto account: (i)
    identificationof prioritiesto be addressed in the National Environmental Policy; (ii) definition
    of inter-sectoralindicators of sustainable development; (iii) analytical studies to review these
    priorities (such as studies on the costs of environmental degradation, and analysis of the
    efficiency, equity and effectiveness of environmental policies); (iv) institutionalization of an
    evaluation and follow-up program for regionalenvironment management, based on national
    environmental priorities; (v) mechanisms to reinforce adequate participation of the different
    ministries in the National Environmental Council, as a forum to discuss priorities; and (vi)
    promotion of institutional learning (e.g., mechanisms for a periodic review of the
    implementationof environmentalpolicies, in order to learn from these experiences).


A.       Improvementof the Efficiency and Effectiveness of the SlNA
1.       ReinforcingEnvironmental Governance

49. To guarantee integrationof the country's National Environment Policy at local level, i.e., the
    CARS, the National Government has established a program to reinforce environmental
    governance. This is based on: (i) accountability of government organizations within the
    SINA, in achieving set goals, and (ii) improvement in the selection process for CAR
    Directors,through an open public election process.

50. RegardingCAR accountability, the Colombian Government issued Decree No.1200 in 2004,
    as a condition of the Policy Matrix for the SusDevDPL. The Decree establishes the
    methodologyfor developing Three-Year Action Plans (PATS),and a system of indicators to
    measurethe resultsand impact of those entities executing National EnvironmentalPolicy, in


                                                   94

     accordance with the Millennium Development Goals. This Decree establishes that MAVDT
     will compile a yearly index of CARs' performance, to evaluate their environmental
     management achievements, the impact perceived by the local populationon their quality of
     life, and the conditions of regional development. The importance of these indicators lies in
     the relationship that can be made between CAR actions and investments as well as their
     socio-economic impact.

51. A fundamental part of applying this environmental framework involves setting up a
     Monitoring and Evaluation System for the CARs, in their execution of the country's National
     Environmental Policy. The System implemented contains consolidated information up to
     December 2003. The System has been used to carry out a Frontier Efficiency Analysis, in
     order to compare the efficiency of the CARs nationwide. Indicators will be used in the
     application of the DEA,I4measuring the output and impact of environmental management.
     Using this framework, the National Governmentwill be able to assess impact by measuring
     environmental management against a set of objectives regarding sustainable development
     and associated indicators.

52. The central reference point of the CARs' Monitoring and Evaluation System is the set of
     indicators that will measuring the results and impact of environmental management carried
     out by the CARs, in terms of environmental improvements as well as improvements in
     socio-economic variables. As mentioned above, these indicators are taken from Decree
     No.1200 of 2004.

53.The Colombian Government will prioritize the development of planning and evaluation
     instruments for urban environmental management; the optimization of management
     oversight instruments for the Regional Autonomous Corporations (CARs), such as Three-
     Year Action Plans (PATS) and Regional Environmental Management Plans (PGARs); the
     development and regional implementation of instrumentsto evaluatethe performanceof the
     CARs; and an analysis of the informationinfrastructurewithin these institutions.To this end,
     action has been taken to reinforce the information technology infrastructure for the
     Informationand Planning Systemfor EnvironmentalManagement (SIPGA).

54. Presidential Decree No.3345, signed in November 2003, established guidelines to ensure
     an open public election process for CAR Directors. This process obliges the Administrative
     Council of the respective Corporation to evaluate each applicant in terms of merit,
     capability, experience and required aptitude, as well as organizing an interview and review
     of the person's educational and professional background. The aim of this process, applied
     during the first semester of 2004 to all CARs within the SINA, is to select the most ideal
     candidatesto fill the posts of Director. As a result of applying this regulation,the country has
     witnessed a more transparent process.




l4 Data Envelopment Analysis (Frontier Analysis) is a comparison tool to define relative efficiency based on
operations research. The comparison is made by contrasting products that are obtained by means of a determined
utilization of input.


                                                        95

2.     Improving Participationinthe Definition of Regulationsand Policies

55. To ensure more effective participation by the most vulnerable groups (those primarily
    affected by environmental degradation), the National Government will issue a Decree to
    optimize the Ministry of Environment's Technical Advisory Council on Environmental Policy
    and Standards (CTA), as a forum for trans-sectoral coordination and to encourage
    participation.

56. MAVDT created the CTA in response to Article 11 of Law 99 (passed in 1993). The
    Council's primary function is to advise the Minister of Environment, Housing and Regional
    Development (MAVDT) on policy formulation and the setting of environmental standards for
    the country. The Council comprises a total of five (5) members, assigned as follows: two (2)
    representatives from public and private universities, and three (3) representatives from the
    councils representing the industry, agriculture, and mining and hydrocarbon sectors (one
    representative per sector).

57. Discussions in the Council, and its recommendations made to MAVDT, have revealed a
    marked imbalance, as there is no representation from other key stakeholders who should
    also be consulted (especially those directly affected by priority environmental problems that
    MAVDT's standards are intended to ameliorate). One of the new Decree's main aims is,
    therefore, to guarantee active participation at the Technical Committee level, by civil society
    representatives who may find themselves affected, and/or who may have a valid interest in
    the attention being paid to environmental problems related to policies or regulations
    submitted for the Council's consideration. At future discussions held by the Council,
    therefore, there is to be a more equitable and balanced participation by stakeholders.


3.      Compositionof the SlNA Group

58. To improve coordination between MAVDT and the CARs, Resolution No.0449 was adopted
    in 2004, to create the SlNA Working Group. The main functions of this Group are: (i) to
    represent the Minister of Environment, Housing and Regional Development in the
    Administrative Councils of the Regional Autonomous Corporations (CARs); (ii) to support
    and coordinate the Planning processes of the CARs, in coordination with the Office of
    Planning, Information and Regional Coordination; (iii) to report on the execution of CARs'
    Plans and Programs; and (iv) to organize and coordinate with the different areas of the
    Ministrythe technical and administrativesupport given to the CARs.

59. As part of its work to date, the SlNA Working Group has adopted and implemented
    mechanisms to improve coordination and direct participation with the CARs, in their
    definition of environmental management priorities under the framework of the Three-Year
    Action Plans (PATS). The SlNA Group's partnership and active participation in PAT
    preparation have ensured that investments and actions outlined in these plans are
    congruentwith national environmental policy priorities.

4.      InstitutionalStrengtheningof MAVDT and DNP

60. Under its structural and institutionalreform strategy, and to focus its actions more closely on
    sustainabledevelopment, MAVDT has begun developingseveral social and economic policy
    documents (CONPES). These policies will require adequate instrumentsfor their oversight.



                                                    96

    At the same time, MAVDT has been supporting the definition and implementation of new
    sectoral policies that call for unified criteria in their formulation. DNP and MAVDT will need
    to establishjoint oversight mechanisms, to ensure the implementationof these new sectoral
    policy measures. As such, MAVDT and DNP are in the process of developing an
     InstitutionalStrengthening Programto meet these requirements.

61 The actions within this Program must aim to consolidate MAVDT and DNPfunctions, and to
    strengthen institutional capacity to oversee sector policies. The following three strategic
    actions have therefore been suggested: (i) strengthening MAVDT'sorganizationalcapacity,
    and ability to develop and implement strategic policies; (ii) strengthening oversight of the
     implementationof National Policieson the Environment and Regional Development; and (iii)
    strengthening MAVDT's processes of coordination and information-sharingwith the entities
     responsible for the implementation of strategic policies and standards. Some of these
    strengtheningactivities will be financed under the proposed IDS Loan.

5.        Improvement in the EnvironmentalLicensingScheme

62.The NationalGovernment plansto issue a new Decreecontaining a proposalfor design and
    adjustment to the environmental licensing scheme. This regulationwill include measures of
     institutional, legal and regulatory reform to guarantee improvements in the efficiency and
     effectiveness of the Environmental Impact Assessment (EIA), in order to open up the
     Government's decision-makingprocess to public scrutiny.


63. Most noteworthy in the provisions are plans: (i) to improve public participation processes in
     designing, in a more expeditious, efficient and objective manner, terms of reference for
     environmental studies; and (ii) for closer congruity between the instrument of environmental
     licences and current legislationconcerningterritorial and/or sectoral planning.

B.        INTEGRATING PRINCIPLES OF SUSTAINABLE DEVELOPMENTSTRATEGIC SECTORS     IN


64. Bearing in mind that the costs of environmental degradation in Colombia are directly related
     to policiesfor sectors strategic to the country'seconomic development, and relatedto urban
     development projects planned by regional authorities, the Government will utilize the
     SusDevDPL to implement a series of measures covering legal, regulatory and policy
     development regarding integrated water management, control of air pollution, and the
     disposal of solid wastes.15


1,        IntegratedWater Management

65. The Reform Program considers the main focus of Integrated Water Management to be
       the preparation of a Water Bill that will be presented to Colombia's Congress. This
       proposed Law must define a regime for water administration, based on the following
       themes: (i) allocation of Water Rights; (ii) water pollution control and water quality; (iii)


  One example of this relationship is the incidence of the use of fossil fuels (carbon, biomass, gasoline, diesel and
other petroleum derivatives used in industry), with emphasis on mobile sources (vehicles, specifically those
operating on diesel, becauseof its viscosity and greater sulphur concentration). This is one of the major areas where
air quality levels are exceeded, bearing inmindpriority parameters such as air particles (PM 2.5). Consumption of
these fuels depends to a large degree on policy guidelines and price signals from the energy sector.



                                                             97

        regulation regarding the management of urban drainage areas and areas subject to
        flooding; (iv) reduction of vulnerabilityto natural disasters; (v) management of marine and
        coastal resources;and (vi) conservationof ecosystemsvital to the water supply.

66. This Law intends to rationalize Colombia's legislation on water, currently addressed in
        several laws, and thus bring the water administration regime into line with the country's
        new constitutional, political and administrative reality. The Law will also seek to cover
        previously identified vacuums and needs regarding updating of the norms, such as the
        theme of economic and financial instruments required for water management, and for
        management of marine waters and the coastal regions. The Law will also seek to
        establish general guidelines for more harmonious development and application of the
        different types of regulatory instruments applicable to water administration (e.g.,
        command and control instrumentsvs. economic instruments).

67. To support the Law's development, regulatory Decrees will be issued covering standards
        of water quality (for human consumption, recreational use and irrigation), standards for
        waste water discharges, and new tariff methodologiesfor pollution fees. The Government
        will also study alternatives designed to optimize the organization of the Department of
        PotableWater and Basic and EnvironmentalSanitation at MAVDT, to bring it into line with
        the new guidelines in the General Water Law, and to ensure a workable institutional
        framework for implementationof the Law.

68. Through its National Council of Economic and Social Policy (CONPES), the National
        Government will issue a policy on water pollution control that is equitable, efficient and
        effective, particularly directed towards controlling spills and/or pollutants that cause
        greater social, economic and environmental cost to Colombia. This policy will be
        complementary to an integrated Environmental Health Policy to be submitted to the
        CONPES for considerationwithin the framework of the SusDevDPL.

69. To complementthe programs and measures put forward by MAVDT, in relation to drinking
        water and basic sanitation, the Government will implement a program promoting good
        hygiene practices, which have been effective in other countries.16(The program will use
        proceeds from the proposed IDS Loan.) The program aims to reduce the incidence of
        water-borne diseases in children under five (5). Promotion of the program will be
        addressed to the public, in general, as well as to specific groups of the public, through
        education campaigns and through direct training (e.g., from leaders, teachers, officials).
        The program will include hand-washingand other hygiene measures or practices integral
        to the successful national experience already demonstrated elsewhere in the reduction of
        water-borne diseases.

70. The proposed IDS Loan will be used to finance the development of a Strategic
        Environmental Assessment (SEA) for environmental health, to analyze the process of
        formulating policies, plans and programs aimed at reducing health risk factors due to
        environmentalvariables.


   Studies mention that correct hand-washing can reduce morbidity due to diarrhoeal diseases by 35% (Esrey et al.
1991, Hutley et al. 1997), by 47% (Curtis, London School of Hygiene and Tropical Medicine), and by 52% (even at
water pollution levels of 10,900 colonies of faecal coliforms per IOOml, Centers for Disease Control, USA).
Initiatives to promote hand-washing using soap, are under way in Ghana, Senegal, Nepal, Indonesia, Madagascar and
Peru.


                                                           98

2.            Sustainable Urban Development Policy

71. The Reform Program includes an Action Plan for the implementation of "Guidelines for
       Optimizing Urban Development Policy (CONPES Document No. 3305, issued in August
       200417). This Action Plan comprises two main components, which will be financed from
       IDS resources: (i) improving the quality of urban life, and (ii) strengthening the
       managementof urban development.

72. In the component referring to Improving the Quality of Urban Life, the Action Plan
       envisions a series of activities under three strategic headings: (i) integrated improvement
       of human settlements, (ii) promotion of mechanisms for the generation and sustainability
       of public spaces, and (iii) improvement of mobility within cities. The first heading covers
       the development of standards and guidelines for the inter-sectoral coordination of public
       service infrastructure (e.g., water, drainage, roads, public transport, equipment); the
       definition of mechanisms to promote and regulate the operation of urban drainage
       infrastructure; programs to improve the quality of urban life at the local level; regional
       workshops, and an international forum on appropriate practices for integrated habitat
       improvement. The second heading covers the consolidationof basic indicators regarding
       public spaces, a review of the standards framework, plus identification and design of
       programsfor public spaces in two municipalities.The third headingcovers the financing of
        management instruments applicable to the Integrated Public Transport Systems (SITM),
       and design of a methodologyto integrate mobility plans into Regional Development Plans
        (POTS).

73. In the component aimed at Strengthening the Management of Urban Development, the
       Action Plan intends to invest in reinforcing both institutionaland private management.The
        main areas to be financed are: (i) urban renovation and redensification, through the                      ,
        definition of a methodology for assessing property values, design of an alternative
        standard for this theme, and promotion of private management in renovation and
        redensification projects; (ii) improvement in the quality of areas of urban expansion,
        through a methodological guide providing procedures and responsibilities of the CARS
        and other environmental authorities, in their evaluation of partial expansion plans; and (iii)
        regulation of the Law on Regional Development, soon to be addressed in pertinent
        decrees.

3.        ControllingAir Pollution

74. As part of the SusDevDPL's policy measures, a policy document (CONPES) will be
        designed and approved during the first semester of 2005 for the implementation of an
        Action Plan to formulate a policy on air pollution control. The Action Plan will emphasize:
        (i) the measurement of key pollutants known to cause severe health problems (e.g., PM
        2.5); (ii) research aimed at a better understandingof the causal relationship between the
        impact on health (e.g., cardiovascular disease, lung cancer, chronic lung diseases, acute
        respiratory infections) and the pollutants; and (iii) promotion of measuresto mitigate these
        impacts on health in urban and rural environments. After this research is conducted, a


"These guidelines point towards the formationof compact cities to guarantee their environmentalquality and
minimum urban conditions. This would include the following strategies: a drive towards urban renovation and
redensification, an improvement in the condition of precarious settlements, an improvement in the mobility within
cities, prevention and mitigation of urban environmental risks, an improvementin the quality of areas covered by
urbanexpansion, and institutionaland regulatoryadjustment.


                                                           99

   proposed National Policy on Air Pollution Control will be submitted to the CONPES for its
   approval. To develop this policy, a National Technical Inter-SectoralCommission for Air
   Pollution Control and Preventionwill be formed, which will be presided over by the Minster
   of MAVDT, or hidher delegate, and comprised of representatives of the Ministries of
   Transport, Mines and Energy, Environment, Housing and Regional Development, and
   Social Protection, as well as IDEAM and DNP. This Commission will advise and provide
   necessary recommendations to the different sectors in order to ensure the effective
   implementation of policies and strategies on air pollution control and prevention that
    MAVDTadopts.

75. CONPES Policy Document Number 3344, of March 2005, establishes a package of
    measures to be considered by the National Technical Inter-SectoralCommission during
   the next two years, with the objective of developing a national policy for the prevention
   and control of air pollution. Among these measures are: (i) the design of an institutional
   strengthening program to provide environmental and sectoral authorities with the
    necessary capacity to carry out their functions related to air pollution prevention, control
   and monitoring; (ii) the strengthening the air quality monitoring program at the national,
    regional and local levels; (iii) the collection of information regarding the environment,
    energy, transport and health sectors in order to improve knowledge and orient
    investigations on the origin and effects of air pollution; (iv) the design of proposals to
    include prevention and control measures in policies and regulationsunder the jurisdiction
    of the Ministries of Transport and Mines and Energy, including regulations related to
    defining instruments that affect energy prices and imports, as well as those related to
    renovating the public automotive park and the importation of vehicles and technologies;
    (v) an evaluation of environmental command and control instruments, with a view to
    improve their effectiveness and efficiency; (vi) the identification of information needs,
    weaknesses and technical requirements in order to strengthen the oversight of air
    pollution-related health effects; and (vii) a review of legislation and strategies related to
    regional planning, urban development and city mobility, in an effort to propose provisions
    to MAVDT that will ensure the inclusion of air pollution prevention considerations in such
    legislationand strategies.

76. On the subject of regulation,the Government will work on issuing decrees to establish and
    implement a new framework to control air pollution. The aim will be to change behavior
    patterns in order to reduce air pollution in urban environments. In principle, these
    standards will include: the National Air Quality Standard and the National Standard for
    EnvironmentalNoise.

77. Using proceeds from the IDS Loan, the Government intends to carry out the following: (i)
    strengthening air quality networks, and (ii) strengthening air quality management.
    Strengthening air quality networks will include financing a diagnostic of the state of 7
    monitoring networks, and validation and adjustment of the air quality monitoring protocol
    established by IDEAM. Also to be financed will be the design of software for information
    sharing and for the generation of a National Unified Report, as well as data analysis; plus
    the acquisition, installationand/or supply of equipment for network operation over a limited
    period of time. The equipment will be primarily for PM 10 and PM 2.5 monitoring,
    automatic gas and/or particle analyzers, and passive samplers.

78. Strengthening of air quality management in terms of prevention and control will assign
    priority to: (i) raising awareness of emission sources through the characterization of
    particulate matter, information processing and analysis, plus compilation, validation,
    adjustment and implementation of an emissions inventory protocol in 7 cities around the



                                                  100

   country; (ii) analyzing meteorological conditions that affect dispersion of pollutants in the
    major cities, for which the appropriate authority will purchase vertical profile evaluation
   equipment (Captive Probe); (iii) raising awareness of the impact of air pollution on health,
   as well as strengthening instruments and mechanisms for institutional management
   through the design of incentives aimed at controlling air pollution in the major urban
   centers, and (iv) the development of a national guide for air quality, plus diagnosis of the
   environmental, health, transport and energy information systems. Also utilizing IDS
    resources, the Government will develop a Strategic Environmental Assessment for the
    nation'sfuel policy.

4.    Final Disposalof Solid Wastes

79. The National Government will issue a Decree and related standards to regulate the final
    disposal of solid wastes in Colombia, consistent with the principles set out in Law 99
    (passed in 1993) and Law 142 (passed in 1994). Using IDS resources, the Government
    will support the development of regulations associated with this Decree, particularly
   directed towards technical specifications and legal, economic and financial instrumentsfor
    the closure of open dumps and the promotion of regional sanitary landfills. The
    Government will also ensure provision of technical assistance to 200 municipalities, by
    means of workshops, technical assistance and publicity materials regardingthe closure of
   the open dumps.

80. The Government will also seek to implement low-cost, high-impact actions, oriented
    towards reducing the impact on public health (reduction in morbidity and mortality levels)
    in problems associated with pollution due to inadequate handling of solid wastes. These
    actions will include generating institutional capacity in the municipalities, departments and
    CARS for integrated management of solid wastes. Among the activities to be financed,
    are: (i) carrying out applicability studies on the Decree addressing final disposal of solid
    wastes; (ii) carrying out a socialization of the Decree; (iii) producing training material on
    integrated solid waste management, and on the application of the Decree; (iv) providing
    training on the Decree; (v) carrying out inter-institutional coordination for the
    implementation of the Decree and follow-up of the resulting impacts; (vi) designing and
    providing technical assistance for a pilot project supporting Integrated Solid Waste
    Management Plans (PGIRS); and (vii) ensuring institutionalstrengthening needed for the
    development and oversight of PGIRS.

ANNEX 3:        FUNDRELATIONSNOTE
                                                                         International Monetary Fund

May 2,2005                                                               700 19thStreet, NW
                                                                         Washington, D.C.20431 USA


                              Colombia: FundRelations Note


On May 2,2005 the Executive Boardof the InternationalMonetary Fund(IMF) approved an 18-
month, SDR 405 million (about US$613 million) Stand-By Arrangementto support Colombia's
economic program through November 2006 (see Press ReleaseNo. 05/95). An amount
equivalent to SDR 193.5 (about US$292.2 million) will be made available, with the remaining
balancedistributed infive equal instalments of SDR42.3 million (about US$64 million).
However, the authorities intendto treat the arrangement as precautionary, and therefore do not
intendto draw on the credit available.

The Board also completed the fourth and final review undera SDR 1.5 billion (about US$2.34
billion) Stand-By Arrangement that was approvedon January 15,2003 (see Press ReleaseNo.
03/04), and which will end effective May 2,2005 at the authorities' request. Incompleting the
review, the Boardapproved Colombia's request for waivers of the non-observanceof two
performancecriteria. No drawings were made under the expiring arrangement either.

Executive BoardAssessment at the Completion of the FourthReview and the approval of a new
Stand-By Arrangement.

"Colombia's economic performance continued to improve during2004, reflecting strong policy
implementation as well as the favorable external environment. Economic activity showed solid
growth, unemployment and poverty fell, and inflation declined to the lowest level indecades.
The external sector strengthened, aided bybroad-basedexport growth as well as sizable net
capital inflows. The combined public sector deficit fell sharply in2004, supportedby
unexpectedly high world oil prices as well as a large surplus of the autonomous local and
regional governments. This helpedreducethe public debt further.

"For 2005-06, economic policies seek to sustainthis strong performance. The successor 18-
month Fundarrangementwill allow for a gradual exit from Fundfinancial support. Fiscalpolicy
continues to aim at reducing the public debt. Inline with this objective, the government is
targeting a decline inthe combined public sector deficits in 2005 and 2006, basedon prudent
assumptions about the world oil price and the overall balance of the local andregional
governments. The authorities also intendto save most of any oil price windfall. Monetary policy
will target a gradualdecline ininflation in2005-06, inthe context of a managedfloat exchange
ratepolicy.

"Structural reforms continue to advance. The authorities intendto press for congressional
approval by June 2005 of the revised budget code, the constitutional amendmentfor pension
reform, and the new securities market law. The authorities are continuing to strengthenthe
financial system. Inparticular, one state-ownedbank was restructured inMarch 2005,
and another one will be brought to the point of sale byend-2005. The government intends to
continue buildingpolitical support for key medium-termreforms, such as strengthening tax



                                               107

policy and improvingthe systemof revenue sharing. These policies will help lay the foundation
for continued growth with stable prices over the mediumterm.

Recent Developments

Real GDP increasedsteadily in2003-04, helpingreduce unemployment. Inflationdeclined to the
lowest levelindecades andthe external sector strengthened, with a low current account deficit
andstrong capital inflow. Fiscalconsolidation, together with the real appreciation of the peso,
lowered gross public debt from about 60 percent of GDP at end-2002to about 53 percentby end-
2004, together with a sizable increaseinpublic deposits. Likewise, the financial sector continued
to performwell.
SafeguardsAssessments

Underthe safeguardspolicy, Colombia was subject to asafeguardsassessment with respectto its
current Stand-By Arrangement with the Fund. Staff concluded a safeguards assessment mission
on April 10,2003 and the final report was approved by managementon May 14,2003. The
assessment noted no significant vulnerabilities.


For questionsplease contact Mr.Oscar Hendrick, ext 38494, SouthKentralAmerican I
Division. Western HemisohereDeoartment. IMF.

ANNEX 5-B: COST OFENVIRONMENTAL DEGRADATION

         As a background document for the Country Environmental Analysis, the study "Cost of
Environmental Damage: A Socio-Economic and Environmental Health Risk Assessment"' provides
estimates of social and economic costs of environmental damage for a number of categories, including
inter alia: water, sanitation and hygiene; natural disasters; urban air pollution; agricultural land
degradation; and indoor air pollution. This annex provides a brief summary of the methodologies for
calculating the value of two of the principal causes of environmental damage: water, sanitation and
hygiene and urban air pollution.

         The quantification and monetary valuation of environmental damage involves many scientific
disciplines including environmental, physical and biological, health sciences and epidemiology, and
environmental economics. Environmental economics relies heavily on other fields within economics,
such as econometrics, welfare economics, public economics, and project economics. New techniques
and methodologies have been developed in recent decades to better understand and quantify preferences
and values of individuals and communities in the context of environmental quality, conservation of
natural resources, and environmental health risks. The applied results from these techniques and
methodologies can then be, and often are, utilized by policy makers and stakeholders in the process of
settingenvironmental objectives andpriorities.

         The cost of environmental damage includes many aspects. Some costs are economic. These
include medical treatment costs and lost work days for illnesses associated with environmental pollution;
reduced productivity of agricultural land due to erosion, salinity or other forms of land degradation;
reduced fishery catch due to pollution and overexploitation; and losses in tourism revenues due to
pollution and/or natural resource degradation. Other costs are associated with reduced well-being and
quality of life. These include among others the risk of mortality from pollution; inadequate waste
management; pain and suffering from illhealth and disability; and the loss of recreational quality and
naturalheritage due to degradation of natural resources.

I.WATER,SANITATION,ANDHYGIENE
         This section discusses the linkages between health and water, sanitation and hygiene, the
environmental health situation in Colombia, the methodologies used to estimate health impacts, and the
socio-economic valuation of these health impacts. The mean estimated annual cost associated with
inadequate water supply, sanitation and hygiene total 1,700-2,220 billion Colombian Pesos per year, with
a mean of 1960 billion Colombian Pesos. The cost of health impacts represents an estimated 70 percent
of total meancost and avertive expenditures about 30 percent. Health impacts include both mortality and
morbidity, and avertive expenditures include bottled water consumption, household water filtering, and
householdboiling of drinkingwater. The cost of each of these categories i s presented in Figure 5.1.




 Larsen, Bjom. 2004. "Cost of EnvironmentalDamage: A Socio-Economicand EnvironmentalHealthRisk Assessment".
Paperpreparedfor the CountryEnvironmentalAnalysis. The entire report is available upon request from the Ministry of
Environment, Housing, and Territorial Development, Republic of Colombia, BogotB, Colombia.

a.      Diarrheal Illness
        Inadequate quantity and quality of potable water supply, sanitation facilities and practices, and
hygiene conditions are associated with a variety of illnesses in adults and children. Esrey et a1 (1991)
provides a comprehensive review of studies documenting this relationship for diseases such as
schistosomiasis (bilharzia), intestinal worms, and diarrhea. From a review of studies, Curtis (2002)
reports a mean reduction of 44 percent in cases of severe diarrhea from regular hand washing practices.
While diarrheal illness is generally not as serious as some other waterborne illnesses, it i s more common
and affects a larger number of people.

        Water, sanitation and hygiene factors also influence child mortality. Esrey et a1 (1991) find in
their review of studies that the median reduction in child mortality from improved water and sanitation
was 55 percent. Shi (1999) provides econometric estimates of the impact of potable water and sewerage
connection on child mortality using a data set for about 90 cities around the world. Literacy and
education level is also found to be important for parental protection of child health against environmental
risk factors. Esrey and Habicht (1988) reports from a study in Malaysia that maternal literacy reduces
child mortality by about 50 percent in the absence of adequate sanitation, but only by 5 percent in the
presence of good sanitation facilities. Literacy i s also found to reduce child mortality by 40 percent if
piped water i s present, suggesting that literate mothers take better advantage of water availability for
hygiene purposes to protect child health. Findings from the Demographic and Health Surveys seem to
further confirm the role of literacy in child mortality reduction. Rutstein (2000) provides a multivariate
regression analysis of infant and child mortality in developing countries using Demographic and Health
Survey data from 56 countries from 1986-98. The study finds a significant relationship between infant
and child mortality rates and piped water supply, flush toilet, matemal education, access to electricity,
medical services, oral rehydration therapy (ORT), vaccination, dirt floor in household dwelling, fertility
rates, and malnutrition. Similarly, Larsen (2003) provides a regression analysis of child mortality using
national data for the year 2000 from 84 developing countries representing 95 percent of the total
population in the developing world. A statistically significant relationship between child mortality and
access to improved water supply, safe sanitation, and female literacy i s confirmed.

b.      BaselineHealth Data.
        Colombia has achieved substantial reductions in child morality and diarrheal child mortality.
Child mortality is now about 1/31dlower than in the Latin America and the Caribbean region, and almost
50 percent lower than the average for lower middle-income countries. Baseline healthdata for estimating
the health impacts of inadequate water supply, sanitation and hygiene are presented in Table 5.1. Data
from DANE indicate that about 7.3 percent of child mortality i s from diarrheal illness. The lower bound
for diarrheal mortality @e., 1,450 deaths) i s based on 7.3 percent of official death records, while the


                                                    113

upper bound (i.e., 1,820 deaths) is based on 7.3 percent of total estimated child mortality published by
DANE.^

          For diarrheal morbidity, however, it i s very difficult or practically impossibleto identify all cases
of diarrhea. The main reason i s that a substantial share of cases i s not treated or does not require
treatment at health facilities, and i s therefore never recorded. A second reason i s that cases treated by
private doctors or clinics are most often not reported to public health authorities. Household surveys
therefore often provide the most reliable indicator of total cases of diarrheal illness. Most household
surveys, however, contain only information on diarrheal illness in children. Moreover, the surveys only
reflect diarrheal prevalence at the time of the survey. As there i s often high variation in diarrheal
prevalence across seasons of the year, extrapolationto an annual average will result in either an over- or
underestimate of total annual cases. Correcting this bias i s often difficult without knowledge of seasonal
variations.

          The Colombia Demographic and Health Survey 2000 (DHS 2000) provides data on diarrheal
prevalence in children under the age of five years. It reports that diarrheal prevalence (preceding two
weeks) was 13.9 percent. This rate was used to estimate annual cases per child under-5, and then total
annual cases in all children under-5. The procedure applied i s to multiply the two-week prevalence rate
by 52/2.5 to arrive at an approximation of the annual cases of per child. The prevalence rate was not
multiplied by 26 two-week periods (Le. 52/2), but multiplied by 52/2.5 for the following reason: The
average duration of diarrheal illness is assumed to be 3-4 days.                  This implies that the two-week
prevalence captures a quarter of the diarrheal prevalence in the week prior to and a quarter in the week
after the two-week prevalence period.

          The DHS household survey does not (nor does any other household survey in Colombia) provide
information on diarrheal illness in the population above 5 years of age. However, the National Institute
of Health (INS) has a large database on cases of diarrheal illness for several years reported by each
department in Colombia based on reported information from healthcare facilities. While this database i s
not complete, it provides an indication of the annual incidence of diarrhea per child relative to annual
incidence for the rest of the p~pulation.~       An analysis of the database suggests that diarrheal incidence in
children under 5 years i s up to seven times higher than the incidence in the population above 5 years of
age. It should be noted however that the database contains information on cases of diarrhea treated at
health facilities. In general, the percentage of cases of diarrhea that are treated at health facilities i s
higher among young children that older children and adults. For instance, according to the DHS 2000,
the percentage of cases of diarrhea among 4-year old children is 28 percent lower than among 0-4 year
olds. Thus the incidence ratio of seven, as suggested from the INS database i s likely an overestimate.
The annual cases of diarrhea per person among the population above 5 years of age, presented in Table
5.1, are therefore estimated at 1/7" to (1/0.72)*1/7" of the annual cases per childunder-5.

          Sometimes diarrheal illness requires hospitalization. There are, however, no centralized records
in Colombia that provide data on the annual number of diarrheal hospitalization. An estimate of
hospitalization rate i s therefore taken from evidence in Egypt (Larsen 2004), as presented in Table 5.1.
Likewise, Table 5.1 presents disability-adjusted life years (DALY) per cases of diarrheal illness, which
are used to estimate the number of DALYs lost because of inadequate water supply, sanitation and
hygiene. While the disability weight for diarrheal morbidity i s similar for children and adults (i.e., 0.119
for children under-5 and 0.086 for the rest of the population), and the duration of illness i s assumed to be
the same (i.e., 3-4 days), the DALYs per 100 thousand cases of diarrheal illness i s much higher for

adults. This is because DALY calculations involve age weighting that attaches a low weight to young
